<td style="border:0; padding:0; vertical-align:middle;"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="524" data-file-width="787" decoding="async" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Flag_of_Ottoman.svg/20px-Flag_of_Ottoman.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Flag_of_Ottoman.svg/30px-Flag_of_Ottoman.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Flag_of_Ottoman.svg/40px-Flag_of_Ottoman.svg.png 2x" width="20"/>

<li value="1"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1041539562">.mw-parser-output .citation{word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}</style><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_name"><b><a href="#ref_name">^</a></b> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Βασιλεία Ῥωμαίων</span></span> may be transliterated in Latin as <i><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">Basileia Rhōmaiōn</i></span></i>, literally meaning "Monarchy of the Romans", but commonly rendered "Empire of the Romans".</span></li><li value="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_lifespan"><b><a href="#ref_lifespan">^</a></b> Between 1204 and 1261 there was an <a href="/wiki/Interregnum" title="Interregnum">interregnum</a> when the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a> took control of Constantinople, causing the Byzantine Empire itself to be divided into the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Nicaea" title="Empire of Nicaea">Empire of Nicaea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond" title="Empire of Trebizond">Empire of Trebizond</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Despotate_of_Epirus" title="Despotate of Epirus">Despotate of Epirus</a>. The Empire of Nicaea is considered by historians to be the legitimate continuation of the Byzantine Empire because it managed to retake Constantinople.</span></li><li value="3"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_capital"><b><a href="#ref_capital">^</a></b> Constantinople became the capital of the (united) empire in 330. In 395 the empire was permanently divided in two halves after the death of <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a>.</span></li><li value="4"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_religion1"><b><a href="#ref_religion1">^</a></b> Tolerated after the Edicts of <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Serdica" title="Edict of Serdica">Serdica</a> (311) and <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">Milan</a> (313); <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">state religion</a> after 380.</span></li><li value="5"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_religion2"><b><a href="#ref_religion2">^</a></b> Following the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a> of 1054.</span></li><li value="6"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_population"><b><a href="#ref_population">^</a></b> See <a href="/wiki/Population_of_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Population of the Byzantine Empire">Population of the Byzantine Empire</a> for more detailed figures taken provided by McEvedy and Jones, <i>Atlas of World Population History</i>, 1978, as well as Angeliki E. Laiou, <i>The Economic History of Byzantium</i>, 2002.</span></li>
</ol></div></td></tr></tbody></table>

.navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_the_Byzantine_Empire_sidebar" title="Template:History of the Byzantine Empire sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_the_Byzantine_Empire_sidebar" title="Template talk:History of the Byzantine Empire sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" <p>The <b>Byzantine Empire</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[note 1]</a></sup> also referred to as the <b>Eastern Roman Empire</b> or <b>Byzantium</b>, was the continuation of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in its eastern provinces during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, when its capital city was <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>. It survived the fragmentation and <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall of the Western Roman Empire</a> in the 5th century AD and continued to exist for an additional thousand years until the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">fall of Constantinople</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in 1453. During most of its existence, the empire remained the most powerful economic, cultural, and military force in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. The terms "Byzantine Empire" and "Eastern Roman Empire" were coined after the end of the realm; its citizens continued to refer to their empire simply as the <b>Roman Empire</b>, and to themselves as Romans<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[note 2]</a></sup>—a term which Greeks continued to use for themselves into Ottoman times. Although the Roman state continued and its traditions were maintained, modern historians distinguish Byzantium from its earlier incarnation because it was centered on Constantinople, oriented towards Greek rather than Latin culture, and characterised by <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Christianity</a>.
</p><p>Several events from the 4th to 6th centuries mark the period of transition during which the Roman Empire's <a href="/wiki/Greek_East_and_Latin_West" title="Greek East and Latin West">Greek East and Latin West</a> diverged. <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine I</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 324–337</span>) reorganised the empire, made Constantinople the capital and legalised <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. Under <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 379–395</span>), Christianity became the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a>, and other religious practices <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_pagans_under_Theodosius_I" title="Persecution of pagans under Theodosius I">were proscribed</a>. In the reign of <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 610–641</span>), the empire's military and administration were restructured, and Greek was gradually adopted for official use in place of Latin. The borders of the empire fluctuated through several cycles of decline and recovery. During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 527–565</span>), the empire reached its greatest extent after re-conquering much of the historically Roman western <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean coast</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Exarchate_of_Africa" title="Exarchate of Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, which it held for two more centuries. The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_War_of_602%E2%80%93628" title="Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628">Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628</a> exhausted the empire's resources, and during the <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">early Muslim conquests</a> of the 7th century, it lost its richest provinces, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Syria" title="Roman Syria">Syria</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>. It then lost Africa to the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_dynasty" title="Umayyad dynasty">Umayyads</a> in 698, before the empire was rescued by the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Isaurian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Isaurian dynasty">Isaurian</a> dynasty.
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_dynasty" title="Macedonian dynasty">Macedonian dynasty</a> (9th–11th centuries), the empire expanded again and experienced the two-century long <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Renaissance" title="Macedonian Renaissance">Macedonian Renaissance</a>, which came to an end with the defeat by <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_dynasty" title="Seljuk dynasty">Seljuk Turks</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manzikert" title="Battle of Manzikert">Battle of Manzikert</a> in 1071. Civil wars and the ensuing Seljuk invasion led to the loss of most of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a>. The empire recovered during the <a href="/wiki/Komnenian_restoration" title="Komnenian restoration">Komnenian restoration</a>, and by the 12th century, Constantinople was the largest and wealthiest city in Europe. The empire was delivered a mortal blow during the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a>, when Constantinople was <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Constantinople" title="Sack of Constantinople">sacked in 1204</a> and the territories that the empire formerly governed <a href="/wiki/Partitio_terrarum_imperii_Romaniae" title="Partitio terrarum imperii Romaniae">were divided</a> into competing Byzantine Greek and <a href="/wiki/Frankokratia" title="Frankokratia">Latin realms</a>. Despite the eventual <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Palaiologos_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Palaiologos dynasty">recovery of Constantinople</a> in 1261, the Byzantine Empire remained only one of several small rival states in the area for the final two centuries of its existence. Its remaining territories were progressively annexed by the Ottomans in the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Ottoman_wars" title="Byzantine–Ottoman wars">Byzantine–Ottoman wars</a> over the 14th and 15th centuries. The fall of Constantinople to the Ottoman Empire in 1453 marked the end of the Byzantine Empire. Refugees fleeing the city after its capture would settle in Italy and other parts of Europe, helping to ignite the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond" title="Empire of Trebizond">Empire of Trebizond</a> was conquered eight years later, when its eponymous capital surrendered to Ottoman forces after it was <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Trebizond_(1461)" title="Siege of Trebizond (1461)">besieged in 1461</a>. The last of the Byzantine successor states, the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Theodoro" title="Principality of Theodoro">Principality of Theodoro</a>, was conquered by the Ottomans in 1475.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Greeks" title="Names of the Greeks">Names of the Greeks</a></div>
<p>Modern historians generally regard the term "Byzantine" as a label of the later years of the Roman Empire from 1557 onwards, 104 years after the empire's collapse, when the German historian <a href="/wiki/Hieronymus_Wolf" title="Hieronymus Wolf">Hieronymus Wolf</a> published his work <i>Corpus Historiæ Byzantinæ</i>, a collection of historical sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[4]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Kaldellis" title="Anthony Kaldellis">Anthony Kaldellis</a>, Athenian historian <a href="/wiki/Laonikos_Chalkokondyles" title="Laonikos Chalkokondyles">Laonikos Chalkokondyles</a> in the mid 15th century advocated a neo-Hellenic identity of the Romans and was the first to use the term in this way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[5]</a></sup> The term comes from "<a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>", the name of the city to which <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine</a> moved his capital, leaving Rome, and rebuilt under the new name of Constantinople. The older name of the city was rarely used from this point onward except in historical or poetic contexts. The publication in 1648 of the <i>Byzantine du Louvre</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Scriptorum_Historiae_Byzantinae" title="Corpus Scriptorum Historiae Byzantinae">Corpus Scriptorum Historiae Byzantinae</a></i>), and in 1680 of <a href="/wiki/Charles_du_Fresne,_sieur_du_Cange" title="Charles du Fresne, sieur du Cange">Du Cange</a>'s <i>Historia Byzantina</i> further popularised the use of "Byzantine" among French authors, such as <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[6]</a></sup> However, it was not until the mid-19th century that the term came into general use in the Western world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[7]</a></sup> Kaldellis claims this was a result of politics of the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>, which included Greece's <a href="/wiki/Megali_Idea" title="Megali Idea">Megali Idea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Byzantine Empire was known to its inhabitants as the "Roman Empire" or the "Empire of the Romans" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Imperium Romanum, Imperium Romanorum</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek_language" title="Medieval Greek language">Medieval Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Βασιλεία τῶν Ῥωμαίων, Ἀρχὴ τῶν Ῥωμαίων</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Medieval_Greek" title="Romanization of Medieval Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Medieval Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-x-medieval-Latn">Basileia tōn Rhōmaiōn, Archē tōn Rhōmaiōn</i></span>), Romania (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Romania</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek_language" title="Medieval Greek language">Medieval Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Ῥωμανία</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Medieval_Greek" title="Romanization of Medieval Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Medieval Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-x-medieval-Latn">Rhōmania</i></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[note 3]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Res Publica Romana</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek_language" title="Medieval Greek language">Medieval Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Πολιτεία τῶν Ῥωμαίων</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Medieval_Greek" title="Romanization of Medieval Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Medieval Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-x-medieval-Latn">Politeia tōn Rhōmaiōn</i></span>), or in Greek "Rhōmais" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek_language" title="Medieval Greek language">Medieval Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Ῥωμαΐς</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[11]</a></sup> The inhabitants called themselves <i>Romaioi,</i> and even as late as the 19th century Greeks typically referred to <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Modern Greek</a> as <i>Romaiika</i> "Romaic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[12]</a></sup> After 1204 when the Byzantine Empire was mostly confined to its purely Greek provinces, the term <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Greeks" title="Names of the Greeks">'Hellenes'</a> was increasingly used instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the Byzantine Empire had a multi-ethnic character during most of its history<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup> and preserved <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Romano-Hellenistic</a> traditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[15]</a></sup> it became identified by its western and northern contemporaries with its increasingly predominant <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greeks" title="Byzantine Greeks">Greek element</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Libri_Carolini" title="Libri Carolini">Libri Carolini</a></i> published in the 790s made the first mention of the term "Empire of the Greeks" (Latin: <i>Imperium Graecorum</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup> and <i>Imperator Graecorum</i> (Emperor of the Greeks), which was an insult first formally attributed to <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XIII" title="Pope John XIII">Pope John XIII</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup> with western medieval sources thereafter using the same terminology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup> This was done to reestablish equal imperial dignity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> to the <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Empire of the Franks</a><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlewitz33:_Emperor_[[Michael_I_Rangabe|Michael_I]]_recognised_Charlemagne_as_an_"Emperor"._He_refused_to_recognise_him_as_a_"Roman_Emperor"_(a_title_which_Michael_reserved_for_himself_and_his_successors),_instead_recognising_him_as_the_"Emperor_of_the_Franks"_24-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlewitz33:_Emperor_[[Michael_I_Rangabe|Michael_I]]_recognised_Charlemagne_as_an_"Emperor"._He_refused_to_recognise_him_as_a_"Roman_Emperor"_(a_title_which_Michael_reserved_for_himself_and_his_successors),_instead_recognising_him_as_the_"Emperor_of_the_Franks"-24'>[21]</a></sup> and what would later become known as the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>.
</p><p>No such distinction existed in the Islamic and Slavic worlds, where the empire was more straightforwardly seen as the continuation of the Roman Empire. In the Islamic world, the Roman Empire was known primarily as <i><a href="/wiki/Rum_(endonym)" title="Rum (endonym)">Rûm</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup> The name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rum_millet" title="Rum millet">millet-i Rûm</a>, or "<i>Roman nation,</i>" was used by the Ottomans until the 20th century to refer to the former subjects of the Byzantine Empire, that is, the <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy#Christianity" title="Orthodoxy">Orthodox Christian</a> community within Ottoman realms.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="History of the Byzantine Empire">History of the Byzantine Empire</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rome-Capitole-StatueConstantin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Rome-Capitole-StatueConstantin.jpg/200px-Rome-Capitole-StatueConstantin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Rome-Capitole-StatueConstantin.jpg/300px-Rome-Capitole-StatueConstantin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Rome-Capitole-StatueConstantin.jpg/400px-Rome-Capitole-StatueConstantin.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rome-Capitole-StatueConstantin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a> was the first Roman emperor to convert to Christianity and moved the seat of the empire to <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>, renamed <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> in his honour.</div></div></div>
<p>By the third century AD, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_army" title="Roman army">Roman army</a> had conquered many territories covering the Mediterranean region and coastal regions in <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">southwestern Europe</a> and North Africa. These territories were home to many different cultural groups, both urban populations, and rural populations. Generally speaking, the eastern Mediterranean provinces were more urbanised than the western, having previously been united under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_Empire" title="Macedonian Empire">Macedonian Empire</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenisation" title="Hellenisation">Hellenised</a> by the influence of Greek culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrogorsky_1959_21_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ostrogorsky_1959_21-26">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The West also suffered more heavily from the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">instability of the 3rd century</a>. This distinction between the established Hellenised East and the younger Latinised West persisted and became increasingly important in later centuries, leading to a gradual estrangement of the two worlds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrogorsky_1959_21_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ostrogorsky_1959_21-26">[23]</a></sup> An early instance of the partition of the empire into East and West occurred in 286, when Emperor <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/Maximian" title="Maximian">Maximian</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Augustus_(title)" title="Augustus (title)">augustus</a></i> of the West. Just a few years later, in 293, Diocletian created the administrative system known as the <a href="/wiki/Tetrarchy" title="Tetrarchy">Tetrarchy</a> to guarantee security in all endangered regions of his empire. The empire was split into two halves, each ruled by one emperor (<i>augustus</i>). Each co-emperor then appointed a young colleague as <i><a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">caesar</a></i> to share power and eventually to succeed the senior partner. Each tetrarch was in charge of a part of the empire, with the divisions based on geographic regions. This new system only lasted 20 years, as emperors quickly began <a href="/wiki/Civil_wars_of_the_Tetrarchy" title="Civil wars of the Tetrarchy">fighting each other for power</a>. The whole empire was eventually reunited by <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a> in 324.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhoff_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhoff-27">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianisation_and_partition_of_the_empire">Christianisation and partition of the empire</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Constantinian_and_Valentinianic_dynasties" title="Byzantine Empire under the Constantinian and Valentinianic dynasties">Byzantine Empire under the Constantinian and Valentinianic dynasties</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/250px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/375px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG/500px-Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walls_of_Constantinople.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Restored section of the <a href="/wiki/Walls_of_Constantinople" title="Walls of Constantinople">Walls of Constantinople</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman-empire-395AD.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="481" data-file-width="648" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Roman-empire-395AD.svg/250px-Roman-empire-395AD.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Roman-empire-395AD.svg/375px-Roman-empire-395AD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Roman-empire-395AD.svg/500px-Roman-empire-395AD.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman-empire-395AD.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After the death of Theodosius I in 395, the empire was again divided. The west <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">disintegrated in the late 400s</a> while the east ended with the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">fall of Constantinople</a> in 1453. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#85d295; color:black;"> </span> The Western Roman Empire</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ed9595; color:black;"> </span> The Eastern Roman/Byzantine Empire</div></div></div></div>
<p>In 330, Constantine moved the seat of the empire to Constantinople, which he founded as a second Rome on the site of Byzantium, a city strategically located on the trade routes between Europe and Asia and between the Mediterranean and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. Constantine introduced substantial changes to the empire's military, monetary, civil and religious institutions. In regards to his economic policies he has been accused by certain scholars of "reckless fiscality", but the gold <a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">solidus</a> he introduced became a stable currency that transformed the economy and promoted development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esler-1081_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esler-1081-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under Constantine, Christianity did not become the exclusive religion of the state but enjoyed imperial preference since <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great_and_Christianity" title="Constantine the Great and Christianity">he supported it with generous privileges</a>. Constantine established the principle that emperors could not settle questions of doctrine on their own but should instead summon <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_council" title="Ecumenical council">general ecclesiastical councils</a> for that purpose. His convening of both the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Arles" title="Synod of Arles">Synod of Arles</a> and the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> indicated his interest in the unity of the Church and showcased his claim to be its head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B163_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-B163-29">[26]</a></sup> The rise of Christianity was briefly interrupted on the accession of the emperor <a href="/wiki/Julian_(emperor)" title="Julian (emperor)">Julian</a> in 361, who made a determined effort to restore polytheism throughout the empire and was thus dubbed "Julian the Apostate" by the Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowersock199779_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowersock199779-30">[27]</a></sup> However, this was reversed when Julian was killed in battle in 363.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreatrexLieu20021_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreatrexLieu20021-31">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> (379–395) was the last emperor to rule both the Eastern and Western halves of the empire. In 391 and 392 he <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_pagans_under_Theodosius_I" title="Persecution of pagans under Theodosius I">issued a series of edicts essentially banning pagan religion</a>. Pagan festivals and sacrifices were banned, as was access to all pagan temples and places of worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriellWilliams2005105_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriellWilliams2005105-32">[29]</a></sup> The last <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a> are believed to have been held in 393.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrottet2004190_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrottet2004190-33">[30]</a></sup> In 395, Theodosius I bequeathed the imperial office jointly to his sons: <a href="/wiki/Arcadius" title="Arcadius">Arcadius</a> in the East and <a href="/wiki/Honorius_(emperor)" title="Honorius (emperor)">Honorius</a> in the West, once again dividing imperial administration. In the 5th century, the Eastern part of the empire was largely spared the difficulties faced by the West—due in part to a more established urban culture and greater financial resources, which allowed it to placate invaders with <a href="/wiki/Tribute" title="Tribute">tribute</a> and pay foreign mercenaries. This success allowed <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a> to focus on codifying Roman law with the <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Theodosianus" title="Codex Theodosianus">Codex Theodosianus</a></i> and further fortification of <a href="/wiki/Walls_of_Constantinople" title="Walls of Constantinople">the walls of Constantinople</a>, which left the city impervious to most attacks until 1204.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECameron200954,_111,_153_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECameron200954,_111,_153-34">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>To fend off the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>, Theodosius had to pay an enormous annual tribute to <a href="/wiki/Attila" title="Attila">Attila</a>. His successor, <a href="/wiki/Marcian" title="Marcian">Marcian</a>, refused to continue to pay the tribute, but Attila had already diverted his attention to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>. After Attila's death in 453, the Hun Empire collapsed, and many of the remaining Huns were often hired as mercenaries by Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire">Loss of the Western Roman Empire</span></h3>
<p>After the fall of Attila, the Eastern Empire enjoyed a period of peace, while the Western Empire <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">continued to deteriorate</a> with the expanding <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">migration and invasions</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Barbarian_kingdoms" title="Barbarian kingdoms">barbarians</a>, most prominently the <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germanic nations</a>. The West's end is usually dated 476 when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Germanic" title="East Germanic">East Germanic</a> Roman <i><a href="/wiki/Foederati" title="Foederati">foederati</a></i> general <a href="/wiki/Odoacer" title="Odoacer">Odoacer</a> deposed the Western Emperor <a href="/wiki/Romulus_Augustulus" title="Romulus Augustulus">Romulus Augustulus</a>, a year after the latter usurped the position from <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nepos" title="Julius Nepos">Julius Nepos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> In 480 with the death of Nepos, Eastern Emperor <a href="/wiki/Zeno_(emperor)" title="Zeno (emperor)">Zeno</a> became sole claimant to emperor of the empire. Odoacer became <a href="/wiki/King_of_Italy" title="King of Italy">King of Italy</a> and was nominally Zeno's subordinate but acted with complete autonomy, eventually providing support to a rebellion against the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns_1991_65,_76–77,_86–87_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burns_1991_65,_76–77,_86–87-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zeno negotiated with the invading <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">Ostrogoths</a>, who had settled in <a href="/wiki/Moesia" title="Moesia">Moesia</a>, convincing the Gothic king <a href="/wiki/Theodoric_the_Great" title="Theodoric the Great">Theodoric</a> to depart for Italy as <i>magister militum per Italiam</i> ("commander in chief for Italy") to depose Odoacer. By urging Theodoric to conquer Italy, Zeno rid the Eastern Empire of an unruly subordinate (Odoacer) and moved another (Theodoric) further from the heart of the empire. After Odoacer's defeat in 493, Theodoric ruled Italy <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i>, although he was never recognised by the eastern emperors as "king" (<i>rex</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns_1991_65,_76–77,_86–87_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burns_1991_65,_76–77,_86–87-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 491, <a href="/wiki/Anastasius_I_Dicorus" title="Anastasius I Dicorus">Anastasius I</a>, an aged civil officer of Roman origin, became emperor, but it was not until 497 that the forces of Anastasius effectively took the measure of <a href="/wiki/Isaurian_War" title="Isaurian War">Isaurian resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup> Anastasius revealed himself as an energetic reformer and an able administrator. He introduced a new coinage system of the copper <i><a href="/wiki/Follis" title="Follis">follis</a></i>, the coin used in most everyday transactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> He also reformed the tax system and permanently abolished the <a href="/wiki/Collatio_lustralis" title="Collatio lustralis">chrysargyron</a> tax. The state treasury contained the enormous sum of 320,000 lb (150,000 kg) of gold when Anastasius died in 518 (roughly worth US$8.3 billion today).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Justinian_dynasty">Justinian dynasty</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Justinian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Justinian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Justinian dynasty</a></div>

style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justinian" title="Justinian">Justinian</a> (left) and (presumed) general <a href="/wiki/Belisarius" title="Belisarius">Belisarius</a> (right) (Mosaic from <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_San_Vitale" title="Basilica of San Vitale">Basilica of San Vitale</a>, 6th century)</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mosaic_of_Theodora_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_(Ravenna,_Italy).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2070" data-file-width="3620" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Mosaic_of_Theodora_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%2C_Italy%29.jpg/260px-Mosaic_of_Theodora_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%2C_Italy%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Mosaic_of_Theodora_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%2C_Italy%29.jpg/390px-Mosaic_of_Theodora_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%2C_Italy%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Mosaic_of_Theodora_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%2C_Italy%29.jpg/520px-Mosaic_of_Theodora_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%2C_Italy%29.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mosaic_of_Theodora_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_(Ravenna,_Italy).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Empress <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodora_(6th_century)" title="Theodora (6th century)">Theodora</a> and attendants (Mosaic from <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_San_Vitale" title="Basilica of San Vitale">Basilica of San Vitale</a>, 6th century)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3681" data-file-width="5514" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/250px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/375px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/500px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> built in 537, during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minarets</a> were added in the 15th–16th centuries by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_and_Sassanid_Empires_in_600_CE.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="674" data-file-width="1368" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Byzantine_and_Sassanid_Empires_in_600_CE.png/250px-Byzantine_and_Sassanid_Empires_in_600_CE.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Byzantine_and_Sassanid_Empires_in_600_CE.png/375px-Byzantine_and_Sassanid_Empires_in_600_CE.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Byzantine_and_Sassanid_Empires_in_600_CE.png/500px-Byzantine_and_Sassanid_Empires_in_600_CE.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_and_Sassanid_Empires_in_600_CE.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Byzantine Empire in c. 600 during the reign of Maurice. Half of the Italian peninsula and most of southern Hispania were lost, but the eastern borders expanded, gaining land from the Persians.</div></div></div>
<p>The Justinian dynasty was founded by <a href="/wiki/Justin_I" title="Justin I">Justin I</a>, who though illiterate, rose through the ranks of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_army" title="Byzantine army">Byzantine army</a> to become emperor in 518.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup> He was succeeded by his nephew <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> in 527, who may already have exerted effective control during Justin's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M290_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-M290-43">[40]</a></sup> One of the most important figures of late antiquity and possibly the last Roman emperor to speak Latin as a first language,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theinheritance_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-theinheritance-44">[41]</a></sup> Justinian's rule constitutes a distinct epoch, marked by the ambitious but only partly realised <i>renovatio imperii</i>, or "restoration of the empire".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup> Justinian's wife <a href="/wiki/Theodora_(wife_of_Justinian_I)" title="Theodora (wife of Justinian I)">Theodora</a> was particularly influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 529, Justinian appointed a ten-man commission chaired by <a href="/wiki/John_the_Cappadocian" title="John the Cappadocian">John the Cappadocian</a> to revise Roman law and create a new codification of laws and jurists' extracts, known as the "<i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Corpus Juris Civilis</a></i>", or the Justinian Code. In 534, the <i>Corpus</i> was updated and, along with the <a href="/wiki/Novellae_Constitutiones" title="Novellae Constitutiones">enactments promulgated by Justinian after 534</a>, formed the system of law used for most of the rest of the Byzantine era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup> The <i>Corpus</i> forms the basis of <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> of many modern states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 532, attempting to secure his eastern frontier, Justinian signed a peace treaty with <a href="/wiki/Khosrow_I" title="Khosrow I">Khosrau I of Persia</a>, agreeing to pay a large annual tribute to the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sassanids</a>. In the same year, he survived a revolt in Constantinople (the <a href="/wiki/Nika_riots" title="Nika riots">Nika riots</a>), which solidified his power but ended with the deaths of a reported 30,000 to 35,000 rioters on his orders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> The western conquests began in 533, as Justinian sent his general <a href="/wiki/Belisarius" title="Belisarius">Belisarius</a> to reclaim the former province of <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a>, who had been in control since 429 with their capital at <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vandalic_War" title="Vandalic War">Success in the war</a> came with surprising ease, but it was not until 548 that the major local tribes were subdued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ev_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ev-51">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 535, a small Byzantine <a href="/wiki/Gothic_War_(535%E2%80%93554)" title="Gothic War (535–554)">expedition to Sicily</a> met with easy success, but the <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a> stiffened their resistance, and victory did not come until 540 when Belisarius captured <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>, after successful sieges of <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Naples_(536)" title="Siege of Naples (536)">Naples</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Rome_(537%E2%80%93538)" title="Siege of Rome (537–538)">Rome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B180-216_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-B180-216-52">[49]</a></sup> In 535–536, <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">Ostrogoth</a> King <a href="/wiki/Theodahad" title="Theodahad">Theodahad</a> sent <a href="/wiki/Pope_Agapetus_I" title="Pope Agapetus I">Pope Agapetus I</a> to Constantinople to request the removal of Byzantine forces from Sicily, <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia_(Roman_province)" title="Dalmatia (Roman province)">Dalmatia</a>, and Italy. Although Agapetus failed in his mission to sign a peace with Justinian, he succeeded in having the <a href="/wiki/Monophysitism" title="Monophysitism">monophysite</a> Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Anthimus_I_of_Constantinople" title="Anthimus I of Constantinople">Anthimus I</a> denounced at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constantinople_(536)" title="Council of Constantinople (536)">Council of Constantinople</a>, despite Empress Theodora's support and protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maas278T187_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maas278T187-53">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ostrogoths <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(546)" title="Sack of Rome (546)">captured Rome</a> in 546. Belisarius, who had been sent back to Italy in 544, was eventually recalled to Constantinople in 549.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B236-258_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-B236-258-54">[51]</a></sup> The arrival of the Armenian eunuch <a href="/wiki/Narses" title="Narses">Narses</a> in Italy (late 551) with an army of 35,000 men marked another shift in Gothic fortunes. Ostrogoth King <a href="/wiki/Totila" title="Totila">Totila</a> was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Taginae" title="Battle of Taginae">Battle of Taginae</a>, and his successor <a href="/wiki/Teia" title="Teia">Teia</a> was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mons_Lactarius" title="Battle of Mons Lactarius">Battle of Mons Lactarius</a> in October 552. Despite continuing resistance from a few Gothic garrisons and two subsequent invasions by the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alemanni" title="Alemanni">Alemanni</a>, the war for the Italian peninsula was at an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B259-281_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-B259-281-55">[52]</a></sup> In 551, <a href="/wiki/Athanagild" title="Athanagild">Athanagild</a>, a noble from <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigothic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, sought Justinian's help in a rebellion against the king, and the emperor dispatched a force under <a href="/wiki/Liberius_(praetorian_prefect)" title="Liberius (praetorian prefect)">Liberius</a>, a successful military commander. The empire held on to a small slice of the <a href="/wiki/Spania" title="Spania">Iberian Peninsula</a> coast until the reign of <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B86-288_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-B86-288-56">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the east, the <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Persian_Wars" title="Roman–Persian Wars">Roman–Persian Wars</a> continued until 561 when the envoys of Justinian and Khosrau agreed on a 50-year peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup> By the mid-550s, Justinian had won victories in most theatres of operation, with the notable exception of the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, which were subjected to repeated incursions from the <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gepids" title="Gepids">Gepids</a>. Tribes of <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croats" title="Croats">Croats</a> were later resettled in the northwestern Balkans, during the reign of Heraclius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup> Justinian called Belisarius out of retirement and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Melantias" title="Battle of Melantias">defeated the new Hunnish threat</a>. The strengthening of the Danube fleet caused the <a href="/wiki/Kutrigurs" title="Kutrigurs">Kutrigur</a> Huns to withdraw, and they agreed to a treaty that allowed safe passage back across the Danube.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although polytheism had been <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_Christianization_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Historiography of Christianization of the Roman Empire">suppressed by the state</a> since at least the time of Theodosius I in the 4th century, traditional Greco-Roman culture was still influential in the Eastern empire in the 6th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_philosophy" title="Hellenistic philosophy">Hellenistic philosophy</a> began to be gradually amalgamated into newer <a href="/wiki/Christian_philosophy" title="Christian philosophy">Christian philosophy</a>. Philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Philoponus" title="John Philoponus">John Philoponus</a> drew on <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">neoplatonic</a> ideas in addition to Christian thought and <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>. Because of the active paganism of its professors, Justinian closed down the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neoplatonic_Academy" title="Neoplatonic Academy">Neoplatonic Academy</a> in 529. Other schools continued in Constantinople, <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, which were the centres of Justinian's empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup> Hymns written by <a href="/wiki/Romanos_the_Melodist" title="Romanos the Melodist">Romanos the Melodist</a> marked the development of the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">Divine Liturgy</a>, while the architects <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_of_Tralles" title="Anthemius of Tralles">Anthemius of Tralles</a> worked to complete the Church of the <a href="/wiki/Sophia_(wisdom)" title="Sophia (wisdom)">Holy Wisdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, which was designed to replace an older church destroyed during the Nika Revolt. Completed in 537, the Hagia Sophia stands today as one of the major monuments of Byzantine architectural history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup> During the 6th and 7th centuries, the empire was struck by a <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">series of epidemics</a>, which devastated the population and contributed to a significant economic decline and a weakening of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup> Great <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bath_House" title="Bath House">bathhouses</a> were built in Byzantine centres such as Constantinople and Antioch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKazhdan1991_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKazhdan1991-64">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>After Justinian died in 565, his successor, <a href="/wiki/Justin_II" title="Justin II">Justin II</a>, refused to pay the large tribute to the Persians. Meanwhile, the Germanic <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a> invaded Italy; by the end of the century, only a third of Italy was in Byzantine hands. Justin II's successor, <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_II_Constantine" title="Tiberius II Constantine">Tiberius II</a>, choosing between his enemies, awarded subsidies to the <a href="/wiki/Pannonian_Avars" title="Pannonian Avars">Avars</a> while taking military action against the Persians. Although Tiberius' general, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_(emperor)" title="Maurice (emperor)">Maurice</a>, led an effective campaign on the eastern frontier, subsidies failed to restrain the Avars, who <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sirmium" title="Siege of Sirmium">captured the Balkan fortress</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sirmium" title="Sirmium">Sirmium</a> in 582, while the Slavs began to make inroads across the Danube.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Louth_2005_113–115_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Louth_2005_113–115-65">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Maurice succeeded Tiberius and intervened in a Persian civil war, placing the legitimate <a href="/wiki/Khosrow_II" title="Khosrow II">Khosrau II</a> back on the throne, and married his daughter to him. Maurice's treaty with his son-in-law enlarged the territories of the empire to the East and allowed the energetic emperor to focus on the Balkans. By 602, a <a href="/wiki/Maurice%27s_Balkan_campaigns" title="Maurice's Balkan campaigns">series of successful Byzantine campaigns</a> had pushed the Avars and Slavs back across the Danube.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Louth_2005_113–115_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Louth_2005_113–115-65">[62]</a></sup> However, Maurice's refusal to ransom several thousand captives taken by the Avars, and his order to the troops to winter in the Danube, caused his popularity to plummet. A revolt broke out under an officer named Phocas, who marched the troops back to Constantinople; Maurice and his family were murdered while trying to escape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shrinking_borders">Shrinking borders</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Heraclian_dynasty">Early Heraclian dynasty</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Heraclian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Heraclian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Heraclian dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piero_della_Francesca_021.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1895" data-file-width="4096" decoding="async" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Piero_della_Francesca_021.jpg/250px-Piero_della_Francesca_021.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Piero_della_Francesca_021.jpg/375px-Piero_della_Francesca_021.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Piero_della_Francesca_021.jpg/500px-Piero_della_Francesca_021.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piero_della_Francesca_021.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Battle between Heraclius and the Persians. Fresco by <a href="/wiki/Piero_della_Francesca" title="Piero della Francesca">Piero della Francesca</a>, c. 1452</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantiumby650AD.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="423" data-file-width="924" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Byzantiumby650AD.svg/250px-Byzantiumby650AD.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Byzantiumby650AD.svg/375px-Byzantiumby650AD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Byzantiumby650AD.svg/500px-Byzantiumby650AD.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantiumby650AD.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>By 650 (pictured) the empire had lost all its southern provinces, except the <a href="/wiki/Exarchate_of_Africa" title="Exarchate of Africa">Exarchate of Africa</a>, to the Rashidun Caliphate. At the same time the Slavs invaded and settled in the Balkans.</div></div></div>
<p>After Maurice's murder by <a href="/wiki/Phocas" title="Phocas">Phocas</a>, Khosrau used the pretext to reconquer the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia_(Roman_province)" title="Mesopotamia (Roman province)">Roman province of Mesopotamia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup> Phocas, an unpopular ruler invariably described in Byzantine sources as a "tyrant", was the target of a number of Senate-led plots. He was eventually <a href="/wiki/Heraclian_revolt" title="Heraclian revolt">deposed in 610 by Heraclius</a>, who sailed to Constantinople from Carthage with an icon affixed to the prow of his ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the accession of Heraclius, the Sassanid advance pushed deep into the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, occupying <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> and removing the <a href="/wiki/True_Cross" title="True Cross">True Cross</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_War_of_602%E2%80%93628" title="Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628">counter-attack launched by Heraclius</a> took on the character of a holy war, and an <a href="/wiki/Acheiropoieta" title="Acheiropoieta">acheiropoieton</a> image of Christ was carried as a military standard<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> (similarly, when Constantinople was saved from a combined Avar–Sassanid–Slavic <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(626)" title="Siege of Constantinople (626)">siege in 626</a>, the victory was attributed to the icons of the <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">Virgin</a> that were led in procession by <a href="/wiki/Sergius_I_of_Constantinople" title="Sergius I of Constantinople">Patriarch Sergius</a> about the walls of the city).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> The combined forces unsuccessfully besieged the capital between June and July. After this, the Sassanid army was forced to withdraw to <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>. The loss came just after news had reached them of yet another Byzantine victory, where Heraclius's brother <a href="/wiki/Theodore_(brother_of_Heraclius)" title="Theodore (brother of Heraclius)">Theodore</a> heavily defeated the Persian general <a href="/wiki/Shahin_Vahmanzadegan" title="Shahin Vahmanzadegan">Shahin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich93_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich93-72">[69]</a></sup> Following this, Heraclius led an invasion into Sassanid Mesopotamia once again.
</p><p>The main Sassanid force <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Nineveh_(627)" title="Battle of Nineveh (627)">was destroyed at Nineveh</a> in 627, and in 629 Heraclius restored the True Cross to Jerusalem in a majestic ceremony,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup> as he marched into the Sassanid capital of Ctesiphon, where <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_civil_war_of_628%E2%80%93632" title="Sasanian civil war of 628–632">anarchy and civil war reigned</a> as a result of the enduring war. Eventually, the Persians were obliged to withdraw all armed forces and return Sassanid-ruled <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Egypt</a>, the Levant and whatever imperial territories of Mesopotamia and <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> were in Roman hands at the time of an earlier peace treaty in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 595</span>. The war had exhausted both the Byzantines and Sassanids, however, and left them extremely vulnerable to the <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">Muslim forces</a> that emerged in the following years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> The Byzantines suffered a crushing defeat by the Arabs at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Yarmuk" title="Battle of the Yarmuk">Battle of Yarmouk</a> in 636, while <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Ctesiphon_(637)" title="Siege of Ctesiphon (637)">Ctesiphon fell</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a> in 637.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="First_Arab_siege_of_Constantinople_.28674.E2.80.93678.29_and_the_theme_system"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Arab_siege_of_Constantinople_(674–678)_and_the_theme_system">First Arab siege of Constantinople (674–678) and the theme system</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1543" data-file-width="3013" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg/250px-Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg/375px-Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg/500px-Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greekfire-madridskylitzes1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Greek_fire" title="Greek fire">Greek fire</a> was first used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Navy" title="Byzantine Navy">Byzantine Navy</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Arab%E2%80%93Byzantine_wars" title="Arab–Byzantine wars">Byzantine–Arab Wars</a> (from the <a href="/wiki/Madrid_Skylitzes" title="Madrid Skylitzes">Madrid Skylitzes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biblioteca_Nacional_de_Espa%C3%B1a" title="Biblioteca Nacional de España">Biblioteca Nacional de España</a>, Madrid).</div></div></div>
<p>The Arabs, firmly in control of Syria and the Levant, sent frequent raiding parties deep into Asia Minor, and in <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(674%E2%80%93678)" title="Siege of Constantinople (674–678)">674–678 laid siege to Constantinople</a>. The Arab fleet was finally repulsed through the use of <a href="/wiki/Greek_fire" title="Greek fire">Greek fire</a>, and a thirty-years' truce was signed between the empire and the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> However, the Anatolian raids continued unabated and accelerated the demise of classical urban culture, with the inhabitants of many cities either refortifying much smaller areas within the old city walls or relocating entirely to nearby fortresses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> Constantinople shrank substantially from 500,000 inhabitants to just 40,000–70,000, and, like other urban centres, it was partly ruralised. The city also lost the free grain shipments in 618, after Egypt fell first to the Persians and then to the Arabs, and public wheat distribution ceased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The void left by the disappearance of the old semi-autonomous civic institutions was filled by the system called <a href="/wiki/Theme_(Byzantine_district)" title="Theme (Byzantine district)"><i>theme</i></a>, which entailed dividing Asia Minor into "provinces" occupied by distinct armies that assumed civil authority and answered directly to the imperial administration. This system may have had its roots in certain <i>ad hoc</i> measures taken by Heraclius, but over the course of the 7th century it developed into an entirely new system of imperial governance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> The massive cultural and institutional restructuring of the empire consequent on the loss of territory in the 7th century has been said to have caused a decisive break in east Mediterranean <i>Romanness</i>, and that the Byzantine state is subsequently best understood as another successor state rather than a real continuation of the Roman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Heraclian_dynasty">Late Heraclian dynasty</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Twenty_Years%27_Anarchy" title="Twenty Years' Anarchy">Twenty Years' Anarchy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Privil_classe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="485" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Privil_classe.jpg/250px-Privil_classe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Privil_classe.jpg/375px-Privil_classe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f5/Privil_classe.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Privil_classe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Constantine_IV" title="Constantine IV">Constantine IV</a> and his retinue, mosaic in <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Sant%27Apollinare_in_Classe" title="Basilica of Sant'Apollinare in Classe">Basilica of Sant'Apollinare in Classe</a>. Constantine IV defeated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Arab_siege_of_Constantinople" title="First Arab siege of Constantinople">First Arab siege of Constantinople</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The withdrawal of large numbers of troops from the Balkans to combat the Persians and then the Arabs in the east opened the door for the gradual southward expansion of Slavic peoples into the peninsula, and, as in Asia Minor, many cities shrank to small fortified settlements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> In the 670s, the <a href="/wiki/Bulgars" title="Bulgars">Bulgars</a> were pushed south of the Danube by the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Khazars" title="Khazars">Khazars</a>. In 680, Byzantine forces sent to disperse these new settlements were defeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haldon_1990_66–67_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haldon_1990_66–67-82">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 681, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_IV" title="Constantine IV">Constantine IV</a> signed a treaty with the Bulgar Khan <a href="/wiki/Asparuh_of_Bulgaria" title="Asparuh of Bulgaria">Asparukh</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">new Bulgarian state</a> assumed sovereignty over several Slavic tribes that had previously, at least in name, recognised Byzantine rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haldon_1990_66–67_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Haldon_1990_66–67-82">[79]</a></sup> In 687–688, the final Heraclian emperor, <a href="/wiki/Justinian_II" title="Justinian II">Justinian II</a>, led an expedition against the Slavs and Bulgarians and made significant gains, although the fact that he had to fight his way from <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> to <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a> demonstrates the degree to which Byzantine power in the north Balkans had declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Justinian II attempted to break the power of the urban aristocracy through severe taxation and the appointment of "outsiders" to administrative posts. He was driven from power in 695 and took shelter first with the Khazars and then with the <a href="/wiki/Bulgarians" title="Bulgarians">Bulgarians</a>. In 705, he returned to Constantinople with the armies of the Bulgarian Khan <a href="/wiki/Tervel_of_Bulgaria" title="Tervel of Bulgaria">Tervel</a>, retook the throne and instituted a reign of terror against his enemies. With his final overthrow in 711, supported once more by the urban aristocracy, the Heraclian dynasty came to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Second_Arab_siege_of_Constantinople_.28717.E2.80.93718.29_and_the_Isaurian_dynasty"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Arab_siege_of_Constantinople_(717–718)_and_the_Isaurian_dynasty">Second Arab siege of Constantinople (717–718) and the Isaurian dynasty</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Isaurian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Isaurian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Isaurian dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="423" data-file-width="925" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg/250px-ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg/375px-ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg/500px-ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ByzantineEmpire717%2Bextrainfo%2Bthemes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Byzantine Empire at the accession of <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo III</a>, c. 717. Striped indicates areas raided by the Umayyads.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solidus-Leo_III_and_Constantine_V-sb1504.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Solidus-Leo_III_and_Constantine_V-sb1504.jpg/250px-Solidus-Leo_III_and_Constantine_V-sb1504.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Solidus-Leo_III_and_Constantine_V-sb1504.jpg/375px-Solidus-Leo_III_and_Constantine_V-sb1504.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Solidus-Leo_III_and_Constantine_V-sb1504.jpg/500px-Solidus-Leo_III_and_Constantine_V-sb1504.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solidus-Leo_III_and_Constantine_V-sb1504.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold <i>solidus</i> of <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo III</a> (left), and his son and heir, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_V" title="Constantine V">Constantine V</a> (right)</div></div></div>
<p>In 717 the Umayyad Caliphate launched a <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(717%E2%80%93718)" title="Siege of Constantinople (717–718)">siege on Constantinople</a> which lasted for one year. However, the combination of <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo III the Isaurian</a>'s military genius, the Byzantines' use of Greek fire, a cold winter in 717–718, and Byzantine diplomacy with the Khan Tervel of Bulgaria resulted in a Byzantine victory. After Leo III turned back the Muslim assault in 718, he addressed himself to the task of reorganising and consolidating the <i>themes</i> in Asia Minor. In 740 a major Byzantine victory took place at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Akroinon" title="Battle of Akroinon">Battle of Akroinon</a> where the Byzantines destroyed the Umayyad army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Constantine V won noteworthy victories in northern Syria and also thoroughly undermined Bulgarian strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> In 746, profiting by the <a href="/wiki/Third_Fitna" title="Third Fitna">unstable conditions</a> in the Umayyad Caliphate, which was falling apart under <a href="/wiki/Marwan_II" title="Marwan II">Marwan II</a>, Constantine V invaded Syria and captured <a href="/wiki/Marash" title="Marash">Germanikeia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Keramaia" title="Battle of Keramaia">Battle of Keramaia</a> resulted in a major Byzantine naval victory over the Umayyad fleet. Coupled with military defeats on other fronts of the caliphate and internal instability, Umayyad expansion came to an end.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_dispute_over_iconoclasm">Religious dispute over iconoclasm</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_iconoclasm" title="Byzantine iconoclasm">Byzantine iconoclasm</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Irenekirken.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1798" data-file-width="1332" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Irenekirken.jpg/220px-Irenekirken.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Irenekirken.jpg/330px-Irenekirken.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Irenekirken.jpg/440px-Irenekirken.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Irenekirken.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Simple Cross: An example of <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">Iconoclast</a> art in the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Irene" title="Hagia Irene">Hagia Irene</a> Church in Istanbul.</div></div></div>
<p>The 8th and early 9th centuries were also dominated by controversy and religious division over <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">Iconoclasm</a>, which was the main political issue in the empire for over a century. <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">Icons</a> (here meaning all forms of religious imagery) were banned by Leo and Constantine from around 730, leading to revolts by <a href="/wiki/Iconodulism" title="Iconodulism">iconodules</a> (supporters of icons) throughout the empire. After the efforts of Empress <a href="/wiki/Irene_of_Athens" title="Irene of Athens">Irene</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Second Council of Nicaea</a> met in 787 and affirmed that icons could be venerated but not worshipped. Irene is said to have endeavoured to negotiate a marriage between herself and <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, but according to <a href="/wiki/Theophanes_the_Confessor" title="Theophanes the Confessor">Theophanes the Confessor</a> the scheme was frustrated by <a href="/wiki/Aetios_(eunuch)" title="Aetios (eunuch)">Aetios</a>, one of her advisors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G89_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-G89-87">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 9th century, <a href="/wiki/Leo_V_the_Armenian" title="Leo V the Armenian">Leo V</a> reintroduced the policy of iconoclasm, but in 843 Empress <a href="/wiki/Theodora_(empress)" title="Theodora (empress)">Theodora</a> restored the veneration of icons with the help of <a href="/wiki/Methodios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Methodios I of Constantinople">Patriarch Methodios</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P11_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-P11-88">[85]</a></sup> Iconoclasm played a part in the further alienation of East from West, which worsened during the so-called <a href="/wiki/Photian_schism" title="Photian schism">Photian schism</a> when <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_I" title="Pope Nicholas I">Pope Nicholas I</a> challenged the elevation of <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Photios</a> to the patriarchate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Macedonian_dynasty_and_resurgence_.28867.E2.80.931025.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Macedonian_dynasty_and_resurgence_(867–1025)">Macedonian dynasty and resurgence (867–1025)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Macedonian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Macedonian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Macedonian dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ByzantineEmpire867AD4-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="423" data-file-width="925" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/ByzantineEmpire867AD4-en.svg/280px-ByzantineEmpire867AD4-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/ByzantineEmpire867AD4-en.svg/420px-ByzantineEmpire867AD4-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/ByzantineEmpire867AD4-en.svg/560px-ByzantineEmpire867AD4-en.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ByzantineEmpire867AD4-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Byzantine Empire, c. 867</div></div></div>
<p>The accession of <a href="/wiki/Basil_I" title="Basil I">Basil I</a> to the throne in 867 marks the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_dynasty" title="Macedonian dynasty">Macedonian dynasty</a>, which ruled for 150 years. This dynasty included some of the ablest emperors in Byzantium's history, and <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Renaissance" title="Macedonian Renaissance">the period is one of revival</a>. The empire moved from defending against external enemies to reconquest of territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-95_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-95-90">[87]</a></sup> The Macedonian dynasty was characterised by a cultural revival in spheres such as philosophy and the arts. There was a conscious effort to restore the brilliance of the period before the <a href="/wiki/Slavic_migrations_to_the_Balkans" title="Slavic migrations to the Balkans">Slavic</a> and subsequent Arab invasions, and the Macedonian era has been dubbed the "Golden Age" of Byzantium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-95_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-95-90">[87]</a></sup> Although the empire was significantly smaller than during the reign of Justinian I, it had regained much strength, as the remaining territories were less geographically dispersed and more politically, economically, and culturally integrated.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_against_the_Abbasids">Wars against the Abbasids</span></h4>
