<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arab%E2%80%93Byzantine_wars" title="Arab–Byzantine wars">Arab–Byzantine wars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="581" data-file-width="1304" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png/250px-Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png/375px-Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png/500px-Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leo_Phokas_defeats_Hambdan_at_Adrassos.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The general <a href="/wiki/Leo_Phokas_the_Younger" title="Leo Phokas the Younger">Leo Phokas</a> defeats the <a href="/wiki/Hamdanid_dynasty" title="Hamdanid dynasty">Hamdanid</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Aleppo" title="Emirate of Aleppo">Emirate of Aleppo</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Andrassos" title="Battle of Andrassos">Andrassos</a> in 960, from the <i><a href="/wiki/Madrid_Skylitzes" title="Madrid Skylitzes">Madrid Skylitzes</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Taking advantage of the empire's weakness after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolt_of_Thomas_the_Slav" title="Revolt of Thomas the Slav">Revolt of Thomas the Slav</a> in the early 820s, the Arabs re-emerged and <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Crete" title="Emirate of Crete">captured Crete</a>. They also successfully attacked Sicily, but in 863 general <a href="/wiki/Petronas_(general)" title="Petronas (general)">Petronas</a> gained a decisive victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lalakaon" title="Battle of Lalakaon">Battle of Lalakaon</a> against <a href="/wiki/Umar_al-Aqta" title="Umar al-Aqta">Umar al-Aqta</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">emir</a> of Melitene (<a href="/wiki/Malatya" title="Malatya">Malatya</a>). Under the leadership of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Krum" title="Krum">Krum</a>, the Bulgarian threat also re-emerged, but in 815–816 Krum's son, <a href="/wiki/Omurtag_of_Bulgaria" title="Omurtag of Bulgaria">Omurtag</a>, signed a <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Bulgarian_treaty_of_815" title="Byzantine–Bulgarian treaty of 815">peace treaty</a> with <a href="/wiki/Leo_V_the_Armenian" title="Leo V the Armenian">Leo V</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 830s the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> started military excursions culminating with a victory in the <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Amorium" title="Sack of Amorium">Sack of Amorium</a>. The Byzantines then counter-attacked and <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Damietta_(853)" title="Sack of Damietta (853)">sacked Damietta</a> in Egypt. Later the Abbasid Caliphate responded by sending their troops into Anatolia again, sacking and marauding until they were eventually annihilated by the Byzantines at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lalakaon" title="Battle of Lalakaon">Battle of Lalakaon</a> in 863.
</p><p>In the early years of <a href="/wiki/Basil_I" title="Basil I">Basil I</a>'s reign, Arab raids on the coasts of Dalmatia and the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Ragusa_(866%E2%80%93868)" title="Siege of Ragusa (866–868)">siege of Ragusa (866–868)</a> were defeated, and the region once again came under secure Byzantine control. This enabled Byzantine missionaries to penetrate to the interior and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christianization_of_the_Serbs" title="Christianization of the Serbs">convert the Serbs</a> and the principalities of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Herzegovina" title="Herzegovina">Herzegovina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> to Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-96_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-96-92">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>By contrast, the Byzantine position in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a> was gradually consolidated; by 873 <a href="/wiki/Bari" title="Bari">Bari</a> was once again under Byzantine rule, and most of Southern Italy remained in the empire for the next 200 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-96_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-96-92">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup> On the more important eastern front, the empire rebuilt its defences and went on the offensive. The <a href="/wiki/Paulicianism" title="Paulicianism">Paulicians</a> were defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bathys_Ryax" title="Battle of Bathys Ryax">Battle of Bathys Ryax</a> and their capital of Tephrike (<a href="/wiki/Divri%C4%9Fi" title="Divriği">Divrigi</a>) taken, while the offensive against the Abbasid Caliphate began with the recapture of <a href="/wiki/Samsat" title="Samsat">Samosata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-96_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-96-92">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="458" data-file-width="412" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg/200px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg/300px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg/400px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol1v.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>10th century military successes were coupled with a major cultural revival, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Renaissance" title="Macedonian Renaissance">Macedonian Renaissance</a>. Miniature from the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Psalter" title="Paris Psalter">Paris Psalter</a>, an example of Hellenistic-influenced art.</div></div></div>
<p>Under Basil's son and successor, <a href="/wiki/Leo_VI_the_Wise" title="Leo VI the Wise">Leo VI the Wise</a>, the gains in the east against the enfeebled Abbasid Caliphate continued. <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Taormina_(902)" title="Siege of Taormina (902)">Sicily was lost</a> to the Arabs in 902, and in 904 <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a>, the empire's second city, <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Thessalonica_(904)" title="Sack of Thessalonica (904)">was sacked</a> by an Arab fleet. The naval weakness of the empire was rectified. Despite this revenge, the Byzantines were still unable to strike a decisive blow against the Muslims, who inflicted a crushing defeat on the imperial forces when they attempted to regain <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Crete" title="Emirate of Crete">Crete</a> in 911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B101_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-B101-94">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The death of the Bulgarian Emperor <a href="/wiki/Simeon_I_of_Bulgaria" title="Simeon I of Bulgaria">Simeon I</a> in 927 severely weakened the Bulgarians, allowing the Byzantines to concentrate on the eastern front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-107_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-107-95">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malatya" title="Malatya">Melitene</a> was permanently recaptured in 934, and in 943 the famous general <a href="/wiki/John_Kourkouas" title="John Kourkouas">John Kourkouas</a> continued the offensive in Mesopotamia with some noteworthy victories, culminating in the reconquest of <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a>. Kourkouas was especially celebrated for returning to Constantinople the venerated <a href="/wiki/Image_of_Edessa" title="Image of Edessa">Mandylion</a>, a relic purportedly imprinted with a portrait of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-108_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-108-96">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>The soldier-emperors <a href="/wiki/Nikephoros_II_Phokas" title="Nikephoros II Phokas">Nikephoros II Phokas</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 963–969</span>) and <a href="/wiki/John_I_Tzimiskes" title="John I Tzimiskes">John I Tzimiskes</a> (969–976) expanded the empire well into Syria, defeating the emirs of northwest <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>. Nikephoros <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Aleppo_(962)" title="Sack of Aleppo (962)">took Aleppo</a> in 962, and the Arabs were decisively expelled from Crete in 963. The recapture of Crete in the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Chandax" title="Siege of Chandax">siege of Chandax</a> put an end to Arab raids in the Aegean, allowing mainland Greece to flourish again. <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> was permanently retaken in 965, and the successes of Nikephoros culminated in 969 with the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Antioch_(968%E2%80%93969)" title="Siege of Antioch (968–969)">siege of Antioch</a> and its recapture, which he incorporated as a province of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-112_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-112-97">[94]</a></sup> His successor John Tzimiskes recaptured Damascus, <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acre,_Israel" title="Acre, Israel">Acre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caesarea" title="Caesarea">Caesarea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiberias" title="Tiberias">Tiberias</a>, putting Byzantine armies within striking distance of Jerusalem, although the Muslim power centres in Iraq and Egypt were left untouched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-113_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-113-98">[95]</a></sup> After much campaigning in the north, the last Arab threat to Byzantium, the rich province of Sicily, was targeted in 1025 by <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a>, who died before the expedition could be completed. By that time the empire stretched from the straits of <a href="/wiki/Messina" title="Messina">Messina</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> and from the Danube to Syria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-116_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-116-99">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_against_the_Bulgarian_Empire">Wars against the Bulgarian Empire</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Bulgarian_wars" title="Byzantine–Bulgarian wars">Byzantine–Bulgarian wars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Bulgarian_war_of_894%E2%80%93896" title="Byzantine–Bulgarian war of 894–896">Byzantine–Bulgarian war of 894–896</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Bulgarian_war_of_913%E2%80%93927" title="Byzantine–Bulgarian war of 913–927">Byzantine–Bulgarian war of 913–927</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basilios_II.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="894" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Basilios_II.jpg/200px-Basilios_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Basilios_II.jpg/300px-Basilios_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Basilios_II.jpg/400px-Basilios_II.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basilios_II.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emperor <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 976–1025</span>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1104" data-file-width="1963" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg/250px-Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg/375px-Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg/500px-Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_Byzantine_Empire_1025-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The extent of the Empire under <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a></div></div></div>
<p>The traditional struggle with the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">See of Rome</a> continued through the Macedonian period, spurred by the question of religious supremacy over the <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Bulgaria" title="Christianization of Bulgaria">newly Christianised</a> state of Bulgaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-95_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-95-90">[87]</a></sup> Ending eighty years of peace between the two states, the powerful Bulgarian <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">Tsar</a> <a href="/wiki/Simeon_I_of_Bulgaria" title="Simeon I of Bulgaria">Simeon I</a> invaded in 894 but was pushed back by the Byzantines, who used their fleet to sail up the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> to attack the Bulgarian rear, enlisting the support of the <a href="/wiki/Hungarians" title="Hungarians">Hungarians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup> The Byzantines were defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Boulgarophygon" title="Battle of Boulgarophygon">Battle of Boulgarophygon</a> in 896, however, and agreed to pay annual subsidies to the Bulgarians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B101_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-B101-94">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Leo_VI_the_Wise" title="Leo VI the Wise">Leo the Wise</a> died in 912, and hostilities resumed as Simeon marched to Constantinople at the head of a large army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup> Although the walls of the city were impregnable, the Byzantine administration was in disarray and Simeon was invited into the city, where he was granted the crown of <i>basileus</i> (emperor) of Bulgaria and had the young Emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_VII" title="Constantine VII">Constantine VII</a> marry one of his daughters. When a revolt in Constantinople halted his dynastic project, he again invaded Thrace and conquered <a href="/wiki/Edirne" title="Edirne">Adrianople</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup> The empire now faced the problem of a powerful Christian state within a few days' marching distance from Constantinople,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-95_90-3"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-95-90">[87]</a></sup> as well as having to fight on two fronts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B101_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-B101-94">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>A great imperial expedition under <a href="/wiki/Leo_Phokas_the_Elder" title="Leo Phokas the Elder">Leo Phocas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romanos_I_Lekapenos" title="Romanos I Lekapenos">Romanos I Lekapenos</a> ended with another crushing Byzantine defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Achelous_(917)" title="Battle of Achelous (917)">Battle of Achelous</a> in 917, and the following year the Bulgarians were free to ravage northern Greece. Adrianople was plundered again in 923, and a Bulgarian army laid siege to Constantinople in 924. Simeon died suddenly in 927, however, and Bulgarian power collapsed with him. Bulgaria and Byzantium entered a long period of peaceful relations, and the empire was free to concentrate on the eastern front against the Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup> In 968, Bulgaria was overrun by the <a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus' people">Rus'</a> under <a href="/wiki/Sviatoslav_I" title="Sviatoslav I">Sviatoslav I</a>, but three years later, <a href="/wiki/John_I_Tzimiskes" title="John I Tzimiskes">John I Tzimiskes</a> <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Dorostolon" title="Siege of Dorostolon">defeated the Rus'</a> and re-incorporated eastern Bulgaria into the Byzantine Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bulgarian resistance revived under the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Cometopuli_dynasty" title="Cometopuli dynasty">Cometopuli dynasty</a>, but Emperor <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 976–1025</span>) made the submission of the Bulgarians his primary goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B115_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-B115-105">[102]</a></sup> Basil's first expedition against Bulgaria, however, resulted in a defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Gates_of_Trajan" title="Battle of the Gates of Trajan">Gates of Trajan</a>. For the next few years, the emperor was preoccupied with internal revolts in Anatolia, while the Bulgarians expanded their realm in the Balkans. The war dragged on for nearly twenty years. The Byzantine victories of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Spercheios" title="Battle of Spercheios">Spercheios</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Skopje" title="Battle of Skopje">Skopje</a> decisively weakened the Bulgarian army, and in annual campaigns Basil methodically reduced the Bulgarian strongholds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B115_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-B115-105">[102]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kleidion" title="Battle of Kleidion">Battle of Kleidion</a> in 1014 the Bulgarians were annihilated: their army was captured, and it is said that 99 out of every 100 men were blinded, with the hundredth man left with one eye so he could lead his compatriots home. When Tsar <a href="/wiki/Samuel_of_Bulgaria" title="Samuel of Bulgaria">Samuil</a> saw the broken remains of his once formidable army, he died of shock. By 1018, the last Bulgarian strongholds had surrendered, and the country became part of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B115_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-B115-105">[102]</a></sup> This victory restored the Danube frontier, which had not been held since the days of the Emperor Heraclius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-116_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-116-99">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Relations_with_the_Kievan_Rus.27"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_the_Kievan_Rus'">Relations with the Kievan Rus'</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg/250px-%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg/375px-%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg/500px-%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A6%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B3%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus' people">Rus'</a> under the walls of Constantinople (860)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_body_of_Leo_V_is_dragged_to_the_Hippodrome_through_the_Skyla_Gate.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1230" data-file-width="2245" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/The_body_of_Leo_V_is_dragged_to_the_Hippodrome_through_the_Skyla_Gate.jpg/250px-The_body_of_Leo_V_is_dragged_to_the_Hippodrome_through_the_Skyla_Gate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/The_body_of_Leo_V_is_dragged_to_the_Hippodrome_through_the_Skyla_Gate.jpg/375px-The_body_of_Leo_V_is_dragged_to_the_Hippodrome_through_the_Skyla_Gate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/The_body_of_Leo_V_is_dragged_to_the_Hippodrome_through_the_Skyla_Gate.jpg/500px-The_body_of_Leo_V_is_dragged_to_the_Hippodrome_through_the_Skyla_Gate.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_body_of_Leo_V_is_dragged_to_the_Hippodrome_through_the_Skyla_Gate.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Varangian Guardsmen, an illumination from the <a href="/wiki/John_Skylitzes" title="John Skylitzes">Skylitzis Chronicle</a></div></div></div>
<p>Between 850 and 1100, the empire developed a mixed relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>, which had emerged to the north across the Black Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-114-115_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-114-115-106">[103]</a></sup> This relationship had long-lasting repercussions in the history of the <a href="/wiki/East_Slavs" title="East Slavs">East Slavs</a>, and the empire quickly became the main <a href="/wiki/Route_from_the_Varangians_to_the_Greeks" title="Route from the Varangians to the Greeks">trading and cultural partner</a> for <a href="/wiki/Kyiv" title="Kyiv">Kiev</a>. The Rus' launched their first <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(860)" title="Siege of Constantinople (860)">attack against Constantinople</a> in 860, pillaging the suburbs of the city. In 941, <a href="/wiki/Rus%27%E2%80%93Byzantine_War_(941)" title="Rus'–Byzantine War (941)">they appeared on the Asian shore</a> of the Bosphorus, but this time they were crushed, an indication of the improvements in the Byzantine military position after 907, when <a href="/wiki/Rus%27%E2%80%93Byzantine_Treaty_(907)" title="Rus'–Byzantine Treaty (907)">only diplomacy had been able to push back the invaders</a>. <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a> could not ignore the emerging power of the Rus', and following the example of his predecessors he used religion as a means for achieving political purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_2009_77_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron_2009_77-107">[104]</a></sup> Rus'–Byzantine relations became closer following the marriage of <a href="/wiki/Anna_Porphyrogenita" title="Anna Porphyrogenita">Anna Porphyrogeneta</a> to <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_the_Great" title="Vladimir the Great">Vladimir the Great</a> in 988, and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Kievan_Rus%27" title="Christianization of Kievan Rus'">Christianisation of the Rus'</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-114-115_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-114-115-106">[103]</a></sup> Byzantine priests, architects, and artists were invited to work on numerous cathedrals and churches around Rus', expanding Byzantine cultural influence even further, while numerous Rus' served in the Byzantine army as mercenaries, most notably as the famous <a href="/wiki/Varangian_Guard" title="Varangian Guard">Varangian Guard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-114-115_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-114-115-106">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even after the Christianisation of the Rus', however, relations were not always friendly. The most serious conflict between the two powers was an <a href="/wiki/Sviatoslav%27s_invasion_of_Bulgaria" title="Sviatoslav's invasion of Bulgaria">invasion of Bulgaria in 968</a>, but several Rus' raiding expeditions against the Byzantine cities of the Black Sea coast and Constantinople are also recorded. Although most were repulsed, they were often followed by treaties that were generally favourable to the Rus', such as the one concluded at the end of <a href="/wiki/Rus%27%E2%80%93Byzantine_War_(1043)" title="Rus'–Byzantine War (1043)">the war of 1043</a>, during which the Rus' indicated their ambitions to compete with the Byzantines as an independent power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_2009_77_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron_2009_77-107">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaigns_in_the_Caucasus">Campaigns in the Caucasus</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Georgian_wars" title="Byzantine–Georgian wars">Byzantine–Georgian wars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Seljuk_wars" title="Byzantine–Seljuk wars">Byzantine–Seljuk wars</a></div>
<p>Between 1021 and 1022, following years of tensions, Basil II led a series of victorious campaigns against the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Georgia" title="Kingdom of Georgia">Kingdom of Georgia</a>, resulting in the annexation of several Georgian provinces to the empire. Basil's successors also annexed <a href="/wiki/Bagratid_Armenia" title="Bagratid Armenia">Bagratid Armenia</a> in 1045. Importantly, both Georgia and Armenia were significantly weakened by the Byzantine administration's policy of heavy taxation and abolishing of the levy. The weakening of Georgia and Armenia played a significant role in the Byzantine <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manzikert" title="Battle of Manzikert">defeat at Manzikert</a> in 1071.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RappCrego2018_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-RappCrego2018-108">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Apex">Apex</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bizansist_touchup.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="970" data-file-width="2516" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/320px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/480px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Bizansist_touchup.jpg/640px-Bizansist_touchup.jpg 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bizansist_touchup.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> was the largest and wealthiest city in Europe throughout late antiquity and most of the Middle Ages until the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a> in 1204.</div></div></div>
<p>Basil II is considered among the most capable Byzantine emperors and his reign as the apex of the empire in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. By 1025, the date of Basil II's death, the Byzantine Empire stretched from Armenia in the east to <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">Calabria</a> in southern Italy in the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-116_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-116-99">[96]</a></sup> Many successes had been achieved, ranging from the conquest of Bulgaria to the annexation of parts of Georgia and Armenia, and the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Crete" title="Byzantine Crete">reconquests of Crete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Cyprus in the Middle Ages">Cyprus</a>, and the important city of <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Antioch_(968%E2%80%93969)" title="Siege of Antioch (968–969)">Antioch</a>. These were not temporary tactical gains but long-term reconquests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-96_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-96-92">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Leo VI achieved the complete <a href="/wiki/Basilika" title="Basilika">codification of Byzantine law</a> in Greek. This monumental work of 60 volumes became the foundation of all subsequent Byzantine law and is still studied today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-97-98_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-97-98-109">[106]</a></sup> Leo also reformed the administration of the empire, redrawing the borders of the administrative subdivisions (the <i><a href="/wiki/Theme_(Byzantine_district)" title="Theme (Byzantine district)">themata</a></i>, or "themes") and tidying up the system of ranks and privileges, as well as regulating the behaviour of the various trade guilds in Constantinople. Leo's reform did much to reduce the previous fragmentation of the empire, which henceforth had one centre of power, Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-98-99_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-98-99-110">[107]</a></sup> However, the increasing military success of the empire greatly enriched and gave the provincial nobility more power over the peasantry, who were essentially reduced to a state of serfdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-98-109_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-98-109-111">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the Macedonian emperors Constantinople flourished, becoming the largest and wealthiest city in Europe, with a population of approximately 400,000 in the 9th and 10th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup> During this period, the Byzantine Empire employed a strong civil service staffed by competent aristocrats that oversaw the collection of taxes, domestic administration, and foreign policy. The Macedonian emperors also increased the empire's wealth by fostering trade with Western Europe, particularly through <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_silk" title="Byzantine silk">the sale of silk</a> and metalwork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Split_between_Orthodoxy_and_Catholicism_.281054.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Split_between_Orthodoxy_and_Catholicism_(1054)">Split between Orthodoxy and Catholicism (1054)</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyril-methodius-small.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="377" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cyril-methodius-small.jpg/170px-Cyril-methodius-small.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cyril-methodius-small.jpg/255px-Cyril-methodius-small.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cyril-methodius-small.jpg/340px-Cyril-methodius-small.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyril-methodius-small.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mural of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saints_Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Saints Cyril and Methodius">Saints Cyril and Methodius</a>, 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Troyan_Monastery" title="Troyan Monastery">Troyan Monastery</a>, Bulgaria</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Macedonian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Macedonian dynasty">Macedonian period</a> included events of momentous religious significance. The conversion of the Bulgarians, Serbs and Rus' to Orthodox Christianity drew the religious map of Europe which still resonates today. <a href="/wiki/Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Cyril and Methodius">Cyril and Methodius</a>, two Byzantine Greek brothers from Thessaloniki, contributed significantly to the <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_the_Slavs" title="Christianization of the Slavs">Christianisation of the Slavs</a> and in the process devised the <a href="/wiki/Glagolitic_script" title="Glagolitic script">Glagolitic alphabet</a>, ancestor to the <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic script</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1054, relations between the Eastern and Western traditions of the <a href="/wiki/Chalcedonian_Christianity" title="Chalcedonian Christianity">Chalcedonian</a> Christian Church reached a terminal crisis. Although there was a formal declaration of institutional separation on 16 July, when three papal legates entered the Hagia Sophia during Divine Liturgy on a Saturday afternoon and placed a <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">bull</a> of excommunication on the altar,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup> the so-called Great Schism was actually the culmination of centuries of gradual separation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crisis_and_fragmentation">Crisis and fragmentation</span></h3>
<p>The Byzantine Empire fell into a period of difficulties, caused to a large extent by the undermining of the theme system and the neglect of the military. Nikephoros II, John Tzimiskes, and Basil II shifted the emphasis of the military divisions (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">τάγματα</span></span>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tagma_(military)" title="Tagma (military)">tagmata</a></i>) from a reactive, defence-oriented citizen army into an army of professional career soldiers, increasingly dependent on foreign mercenaries. Mercenaries were expensive, however, and as the threat of invasion receded in the 10th century, so did the need for maintaining large garrisons and expensive fortifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup> Basil II left a burgeoning treasury upon his death, but he neglected to plan for his succession. None of his immediate successors had any particular military or political talent, and the imperial administration increasingly fell into the hands of the civil service. Incompetent efforts to revive the Byzantine economy resulted in severe inflation and a debased gold currency. The army was seen as both an unnecessary expense and a political threat. A number of standing local units were demobilised, further augmenting the army's dependence on mercenaries, who could be retained and dismissed on an as-needed basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PM_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-PM-118">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="705" data-file-width="1709" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg/290px-The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg/435px-The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg/580px-The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_seizure_of_Edessa_in_Syria_by_the_Byzantine_army_and_the_Arabic_counterattack_from_the_Chronicle_of_John_Skylitzes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The seizure of <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a> (1031) by the Byzantines under <a href="/wiki/George_Maniakes" title="George Maniakes">George Maniakes</a> and the counterattack by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuk_Turks" title="Seljuk Turks">Seljuk Turks</a></div></div></div>
<p>At the same time, Byzantium was faced with new enemies. Its provinces in southern Italy were threatened by the <a href="/wiki/Italo-Normans" title="Italo-Normans">Normans</a> who arrived in Italy at the beginning of the 11th century. During a period of strife between Constantinople and Rome culminating in the East-West Schism of 1054, the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_southern_Italy" title="Norman conquest of southern Italy">Normans advanced slowly but steadily</a> into Byzantine Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Reggio_Calabria" title="Reggio Calabria">Reggio</a>, the capital of the tagma of <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">Calabria</a>, was captured in 1060 by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Guiscard" title="Robert Guiscard">Robert Guiscard</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Otranto" title="Otranto">Otranto</a> in 1068. <a href="/wiki/Bari" title="Bari">Bari</a>, the main Byzantine stronghold in <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">Apulia</a>, was besieged in August 1068 and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Bari" title="Siege of Bari">fell in April 1071</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 1053, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_IX_Monomachos" title="Constantine IX Monomachos">Constantine IX</a> disbanded what the historian <a href="/wiki/John_Skylitzes" title="John Skylitzes">John Skylitzes</a> calls the "Iberian Army", which consisted of 50,000 men, and it was turned into a contemporary <a href="/wiki/Droungarios_of_the_Watch" title="Droungarios of the Watch">Drungary of the Watch</a>. Two other knowledgeable contemporaries, the former officials <a href="/wiki/Michael_Attaleiates" title="Michael Attaleiates">Michael Attaleiates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kekaumenos" title="Kekaumenos">Kekaumenos</a>, agree with Skylitzes that by demobilising these soldiers Constantine did catastrophic harm to the empire's eastern defences. The emergency lent weight to the military aristocracy in Anatolia, who in 1068 secured the election of one of their own, <a href="/wiki/Romanos_IV_Diogenes" title="Romanos IV Diogenes">Romanos Diogenes</a>, as emperor. In the summer of 1071, Romanos undertook a massive eastern campaign to draw the Seljuks into a general engagement with the Byzantine army. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manzikert" title="Battle of Manzikert">Battle of Manzikert</a>, Romanos suffered a surprise defeat by <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> <a href="/wiki/Alp_Arslan" title="Alp Arslan">Alp Arslan</a> and was captured. Alp Arslan treated him with respect and imposed no harsh terms on the Byzantines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PM_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-PM-118">[115]</a></sup> In Constantinople, however, a coup put in power <a href="/wiki/Michael_VII_Doukas" title="Michael VII Doukas">Michael Doukas</a>, who soon faced the opposition of <a href="/wiki/Nikephoros_Bryennios_the_Elder" title="Nikephoros Bryennios the Elder">Nikephoros Bryennios</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikephoros_III_Botaneiates" title="Nikephoros III Botaneiates">Nikephoros III Botaneiates</a>. By 1081, the Seljuks had expanded their rule over virtually the entire Anatolian plateau from Armenia in the east to <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a> in the west, and they had founded their capital at <a href="/wiki/Nicaea" title="Nicaea">Nicaea</a>, just 90 kilometres (56 miles) from Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Komnenian_dynasty_and_the_Crusades">Komnenian dynasty and the Crusades</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Komnenos_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Komnenos dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Komnenos dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="299" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg/170px-Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg/255px-Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8c/Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexios_I_Komnenos.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_Komnenos" title="Alexios I Komnenos">Alexios I</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Komnenos" title="Komnenos">Komnenos dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<p>After Manzikert, a partial recovery (referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Komnenian_restoration" title="Komnenian restoration">Komnenian restoration</a>) was made possible by the <a href="/wiki/Komnenos" title="Komnenos">Komnenian dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M124_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-M124-122">[119]</a></sup> During the Komnenian period from about 1081 to about 1185, the dynasty presided over a sustained, though ultimately incomplete, restoration of the military, territorial, economic, and political position of the Byzantine Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-190_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-190-123">[120]</a></sup> Although the Seljuk Turks occupied the heartland of the empire in Anatolia, most Byzantine military efforts during this period were directed against Western powers, particularly the Normans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-190_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-190-123">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The empire under the Komnenoi played a key role in the history of the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> in the Holy Land, which Alexios I had helped bring about, while also exerting enormous cultural and political influence in Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, and the lands around the Mediterranean Sea under John and Manuel. Contact between Byzantium and the "Latin" West, including the <a href="/wiki/Crusader_states" title="Crusader states">Crusader states</a>, increased significantly during the Komnenian period. Venetian and other Italian traders became resident in large numbers in Constantinople and the empire (there were an estimated 60,000 Latins in Constantinople alone, out of a population of three to four hundred thousand), and their presence together with the numerous Latin mercenaries who were employed by Manuel helped to spread Byzantine technology, art, literature and culture throughout the Latin West, while also leading to a flow of Western ideas and customs into the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alexios_I_and_the_First_Crusade">Alexios I and the First Crusade</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chora_Church_Constantinople_2007_panorama_002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1456" data-file-width="2460" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Chora_Church_Constantinople_2007_panorama_002.jpg/250px-Chora_Church_Constantinople_2007_panorama_002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Chora_Church_Constantinople_2007_panorama_002.jpg/375px-Chora_Church_Constantinople_2007_panorama_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Chora_Church_Constantinople_2007_panorama_002.jpg/500px-Chora_Church_Constantinople_2007_panorama_002.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chora_Church_Constantinople_2007_panorama_002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Chora_Church" title="Chora Church">Chora Church</a>, dating from the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Komnenos_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Komnenos dynasty">Komnenian period</a>, has some of the finest Byzantine frescoes and mosaics.</div></div></div>
<p>The Komnenoi attained power under <a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_Komnenos" title="Alexios I Komnenos">Alexios I</a> in 1081. From the outset of his reign, Alexios faced a formidable attack by the Normans under Guiscard and his son <a href="/wiki/Bohemond_I_of_Antioch" title="Bohemond I of Antioch">Bohemund of Taranto</a>, who <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dyrrhachium_(1081)" title="Battle of Dyrrhachium (1081)">captured Dyrrhachium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corfu" title="Corfu">Corfu</a> and laid siege to <a href="/wiki/Larissa" title="Larissa">Larissa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a>. Guiscard's death in 1085 temporarily eased the Norman problem. The following year, the Seljuq sultan died, and the sultanate was split by internal rivalries. By his own efforts, Alexios defeated the <a href="/wiki/Pechenegs" title="Pechenegs">Pechenegs</a>, who were caught by surprise and annihilated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Levounion" title="Battle of Levounion">Battle of Levounion</a> on 28 April 1091.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Br-125">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg/250px-Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg/375px-Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg/500px-Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantiumforecrusades.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Byzantine Empire and the Seljuk <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_R%C3%BBm" title="Sultanate of Rûm">Sultanate of Rûm</a> before the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> (1095–1099)</div></div></div>
<p>Having achieved stability in the West, Alexios could turn his attention to the severe economic difficulties and the disintegration of the empire's traditional defences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birkenmeier_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birkenmeier-126">[123]</a></sup> However, he still did not have enough manpower to recover the lost territories in Asia Minor and to advance against the Seljuks. At the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Piacenza" title="Council of Piacenza">Council of Piacenza</a> in 1095, envoys from Alexios spoke to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">Pope Urban II</a> about the suffering of the Christians of the East and underscored that without help from the West they would continue to suffer under Muslim rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-127">[124]</a></sup> Urban saw Alexios's request as a dual opportunity to cement Western Europe and reunite the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> under his rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-127">[124]</a></sup> On 27 November 1095, Urban called the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Clermont" title="Council of Clermont">Council of Clermont</a> and urged all those present to take up arms under the sign of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">Cross</a> and launch an armed <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a> to recover Jerusalem and the East from the Muslims. The response in Western Europe was overwhelming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Br-125">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexios had anticipated help in the form of mercenary forces from the West, but he was totally unprepared for the immense and undisciplined force that arrived in Byzantine territory. It was no comfort to Alexios to learn that four of the eight leaders of the main body of the Crusade were Normans, among them Bohemund. Since the crusade had to pass through Constantinople, however, the emperor had some control over it. He required its leaders to swear to restore to the empire any towns or territories they might reconquer from the Turks on their way to the Holy Land. In return, he gave them guides and a military escort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A261_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-A261-128">[125]</a></sup> Alexios was able to recover a number of important cities, islands and much of western Asia Minor. The Crusaders agreed to become Alexios' vassals under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Devol" title="Treaty of Devol">Treaty of Devol</a> in 1108, which marked the end of the Norman threat during Alexios' reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A348-358_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-A348-358-129">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="John_II.2C_Manuel_I_and_the_Second_Crusade"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="John_II,_Manuel_I_and_the_Second_Crusade">John II, Manuel I and the Second Crusade</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Second_Crusade" title="Second Crusade">Second Crusade</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa,_Irene.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1408" data-file-width="2886" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG/250px-Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG/375px-Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG/500px-Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa%2C_Irene.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Santa_Sofia_-_Mosaic_de_Joan_II_Comn%C3%A8_i_la_seva_esposa,_Irene.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> (modern Istanbul), depicting <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">Mary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>, flanked by <a href="/wiki/John_II_Komnenos" title="John II Komnenos">John II Komnenos</a> (left) and his wife <a href="/wiki/Irene_of_Hungary" title="Irene of Hungary">Irene of Hungary</a> (right), 12th century</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Byzantine_Empire,_c.1180.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="926" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg/250px-The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg/375px-The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg/500px-The_Byzantine_Empire%2C_c.1180.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Byzantine_Empire,_c.1180.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Byzantine Empire in orange, c. 1180, at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Komnenian_restoration" title="Komnenian restoration">Komnenian period</a></div></div></div>
<p>Alexios's son <a href="/wiki/John_II_Komnenos" title="John II Komnenos">John II Komnenos</a> succeeded him in 1118 and ruled until 1143. John was a pious and dedicated emperor who was determined to undo the damage to the empire suffered at the Battle of Manzikert half a century earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[127]</a></sup> Famed for his piety and his remarkably mild and just reign, John was an exceptional example of a moral ruler at a time when cruelty was the norm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[128]</a></sup> For this reason, he has been called the Byzantine <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>. During his twenty-five-year reign, John made alliances with the Holy Roman Empire in the West and decisively defeated the Pechenegs at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Beroia" title="Battle of Beroia">Battle of Beroia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B90_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-B90-132">[129]</a></sup> He thwarted Hungarian and Serbian threats during the 1120s, and in 1130 he allied himself with <a href="/wiki/List_of_German_monarchs" title="List of German monarchs">German Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Lothair_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Lothair III, Holy Roman Emperor">Lothair III</a> against Norman King <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">Roger II of Sicily</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECinnamus197674–75_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECinnamus197674–75-133">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the later part of his reign, John focused his activities on the East, personally leading <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Seljuk_wars" title="Byzantine–Seljuk wars">numerous campaigns against the Turks</a> in Asia Minor. His campaigns fundamentally altered the balance of power in the East, forcing the Turks onto the defensive, while restoring many towns, fortresses, and cities across the peninsula to the Byzantines. He defeated the <a href="/wiki/Danishmendids" title="Danishmendids">Danishmend Emirate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Malatya" title="Malatya">Melitene</a> and reconquered all of <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>, while forcing <a href="/wiki/Raymond_of_Poitiers" title="Raymond of Poitiers">Raymond of Poitiers</a>, Prince of Antioch, to recognise Byzantine suzerainty. In an effort to demonstrate the emperor's role as the leader of the Christian world, John marched into the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> at the head of the combined forces of the empire and the Crusader states; yet despite his great vigour pressing the campaign, his hopes were disappointed by the treachery of his Crusader allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup> In 1142, John returned to press his claims to Antioch, but he died in the spring of 1143 following a hunting accident.
</p><p>John's chosen heir was his fourth son, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_I_Komnenos" title="Manuel I Komnenos">Manuel I Komnenos</a>, who campaigned aggressively against his neighbours both in the west and in the east. In Palestine, Manuel allied with the Crusader <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">Kingdom of Jerusalem</a> and sent a large fleet to participate in a combined invasion of <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Egypt</a>. Manuel reinforced his position as overlord of the Crusader states, with his hegemony over Antioch and Jerusalem secured by agreement with <a href="/wiki/Raynald_of_Ch%C3%A2tillon" title="Raynald of Châtillon">Raynald</a>, Prince of Antioch, and <a href="/wiki/Amalric_of_Jerusalem" title="Amalric of Jerusalem">Amalric of Jerusalem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-S-135">[132]</a></sup> In an effort to restore Byzantine control over the ports of southern Italy, he sent an expedition to Italy in 1155, but disputes within the coalition led to the eventual failure of the campaign. Despite this military setback, Manuel's armies successfully invaded the southern parts of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Kingdom of Hungary</a> in 1167, defeating the Hungarians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sirmium" title="Battle of Sirmium">Battle of Sirmium</a>. By 1168, nearly the whole of the eastern Adriatic coast lay in Manuel's hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S372_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-S372-136">[133]</a></sup> Manuel made several alliances with the pope and Western Christian kingdoms, and he successfully handled the passage of the crusaders through his empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M67_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-M67-137">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the East, however, Manuel suffered a major defeat in 1176 at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Myriokephalon" title="Battle of Myriokephalon">Battle of Myriokephalon</a> against the Turks. Yet the losses were quickly recovered, and in the following year Manuel's forces inflicted a defeat upon a force of "picked Turks".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B129_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-B129-138">[135]</a></sup> The Byzantine commander <a href="/wiki/John_Komnenos_Vatatzes" title="John Komnenos Vatatzes">John Vatatzes</a>, who destroyed the Turkish invaders at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hyelion_and_Leimocheir" title="Battle of Hyelion and Leimocheir">Battle of Hyelion and Leimocheir</a>, brought troops from the capital and was able to gather an army along the way, a sign that the Byzantine army remained strong and that the defensive programme of western Asia Minor was still successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B196_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-B196-139">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="12th-century_Renaissance">12th-century Renaissance</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_civilisation_in_the_12th_century" title="Byzantine civilisation in the 12th century">Byzantine civilisation in the 12th century</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Komnenian_Byzantine_army" title="Komnenian Byzantine army">Komnenian Byzantine army</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1494" data-file-width="2024" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg/250px-Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg/375px-Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg/500px-Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meister_von_Nerezi_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Lamentation of Christ</i> (1164), a fresco from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_of_St._Panteleimon_(Nerezi)" title="Church of St. Panteleimon (Nerezi)">church of Saint Panteleimon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nerezi" title="Nerezi">Nerezi</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Macedonia" title="North Macedonia">North Macedonia</a>, considered a superb example of 12th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comnenus" title="Comnenus">Komnenian</a> art</div></div></div>
<p>John and Manuel pursued active military policies, and both deployed considerable resources on sieges and city defences; aggressive fortification policies were at the heart of their imperial military policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B185-186_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-B185-186-140">[137]</a></sup> Despite the defeat at Myriokephalon, the policies of Alexios, John and Manuel resulted in vast territorial gains, increased frontier stability in Asia Minor, and secured the stabilisation of the empire's European frontiers. From c. 1081 to c. 1180, the Komnenian army assured the empire's security, enabling Byzantine civilisation to flourish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br1_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Br1-141">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>This allowed the Western provinces to achieve an economic revival that continued until the close of the century. It has been argued that Byzantium under the Komnenian rule was more prosperous than at any time since the Persian invasions of the 7th century. During the 12th century, population levels rose and extensive tracts of new agricultural land were brought into production. Archaeological evidence from both Europe and Asia Minor shows a considerable increase in the size of urban settlements, together with a notable upsurge in new towns. Trade was also flourishing; the Venetians, the <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoese</a> and others opened up the ports of the Aegean to commerce, shipping goods from the Crusader states and Fatimid Egypt to the west and trading with the empire via Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Day-142">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>In artistic terms, there was a revival in <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a>, and regional schools of architecture began producing many distinctive styles that drew on a range of cultural influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diehl_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diehl-143">[140]</a></sup> During the 12th century, the Byzantines provided their model of early <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">humanism</a> as a renaissance of interest in classical authors. In <a href="/wiki/Eustathius_of_Thessalonica" title="Eustathius of Thessalonica">Eustathius of Thessalonica</a>, Byzantine humanism found its most characteristic expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TM_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-TM-144">[141]</a></sup> In philosophy, there was a resurgence of classical learning not seen since the 7th century, characterised by a significant increase in the publication of commentaries on classical works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-190-218_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-190-218-145">[142]</a></sup> Besides, the first transmission of classical Greek knowledge to the West occurred during the Komnenian period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-218_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-218-146">[143]</a></sup> In terms of prosperity and cultural life, the Komnenian period was one of the peaks in Byzantine history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup> and Constantinople remained the leading city of the Christian world in size, wealth, and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup> There was a renewed interest in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">classical Greek philosophy</a>, as well as an increase in literary output in vernacular Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-190-218_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-190-218-145">[142]</a></sup> Byzantine art and literature held a pre-eminent place in Europe, and the cultural impact of Byzantine art on the West during this period was enormous and of long-lasting significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-218_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-218-146">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_disintegration">Decline and disintegration</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Decline of the Byzantine Empire">Decline of the Byzantine Empire</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Angelid_dynasty">Angelid dynasty</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Angelos_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Angelos dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Angelos dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG/250px-Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG/375px-Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG/500px-Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantium-Angeloi.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Byzantium</a> in the late Angeloi period</div></div></div>
<p>Manuel's death on 24 September 1180 left his 11-year-old son <a href="/wiki/Alexios_II_Komnenos" title="Alexios II Komnenos">Alexios II Komnenos</a> on the throne. Alexios was highly incompetent in the office, and with his mother <a href="/wiki/Maria_of_Antioch" title="Maria of Antioch">Maria of Antioch</a>'s Frankish background, his regency was unpopular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich291_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich291-149">[146]</a></sup> Eventually, <a href="/wiki/Andronikos_I_Komnenos" title="Andronikos I Komnenos">Andronikos I Komnenos</a>, a grandson of Alexios I, launched a revolt against his younger relative and managed to overthrow him in a violent <i>coup d'état</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich292_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich292-150">[147]</a></sup> Utilising his good looks and his immense popularity with the army, Andronikos marched to Constantinople in August 1182 and incited a <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_the_Latins" title="Massacre of the Latins">massacre of the Latins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich292_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich292-150">[147]</a></sup> After eliminating his potential rivals, he had himself crowned as co-emperor in September 1183. He eliminated Alexios II and took his 12-year-old wife <a href="/wiki/Agnes_of_France,_Byzantine_Empress" title="Agnes of France, Byzantine Empress">Agnes of France</a> for himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich292_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich292-150">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Andronikos began his reign well; in particular, the measures he took to reform the government of the empire have been praised by historians. According to <a href="/wiki/George_Ostrogorsky" title="George Ostrogorsky">George Ostrogorsky</a>, Andronikos was determined to root out corruption: under his rule, the sale of offices ceased; selection was based on merit, rather than favouritism; officials were paid an adequate salary to reduce the temptation of bribery. In the provinces, Andronikos's reforms produced a speedy and marked improvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M194_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-M194-151">[148]</a></sup> The aristocrats were infuriated against him, and to make matters worse, Andronikos seems to have become increasingly unbalanced; executions and violence became increasingly common, and his reign turned into a reign of terror.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup> Andronikos seemed almost to seek the extermination of the aristocracy as a whole. The struggle against the aristocracy turned into wholesale slaughter, while the emperor resorted to ever more ruthless measures to shore up his regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M194_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-M194-151">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite his military background, Andronikos failed to deal with <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Komnenos_of_Cyprus" title="Isaac Komnenos of Cyprus">Isaac Komnenos</a>, <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_III_of_Hungary" title="Béla III of Hungary">Béla III of Hungary</a> who reincorporated Croatian territories into Hungary, and <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Nemanja" title="Stefan Nemanja">Stephen Nemanja of Serbia</a> who declared his independence from the Byzantine Empire. Yet, none of these troubles would compare to <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_Sicily" title="William II of Sicily">William II of Sicily</a>'s invasion force of 300 ships and 80,000 men, arriving in 1185 and <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Thessalonica_(1185)" title="Sack of Thessalonica (1185)">sacking Thessalonica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich293_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich293-153">[150]</a></sup> Andronikos mobilised a small fleet of 100 ships to defend the capital, but other than that he was indifferent to the populace. He was finally overthrown when <a href="/wiki/Isaac_II_Angelos" title="Isaac II Angelos">Isaac II Angelos</a>, surviving an imperial assassination attempt, seized power with the aid of the people and had Andronikos killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich294-295_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich294-295-154">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>The reign of Isaac II, and more so that of his brother <a href="/wiki/Alexios_III_Angelos" title="Alexios III Angelos">Alexios III</a>, saw the collapse of what remained of the centralised machinery of Byzantine government and defence. Although the Normans were driven out of Greece, in 1186 the <a href="/wiki/Vlachs" title="Vlachs">Vlachs</a> and Bulgars began a rebellion that led to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bulgarian_Empire" title="Second Bulgarian Empire">Second Bulgarian Empire</a>. The internal policy of the Angeloi was characterised by the squandering of the public treasure and fiscal maladministration. Imperial authority was severely weakened, and the growing power vacuum at the centre of the empire encouraged fragmentation. There is evidence that some Komnenian heirs had set up a semi-independent state in <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">Trebizond</a> before 1204.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-155">[152]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Vasiliev_(historian)" title="Alexander Vasiliev (historian)">Alexander Vasiliev</a>, "the dynasty of the Angeloi, Greek in its origin, ... accelerated the ruin of the Empire, already weakened without and disunited within."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VA_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-VA-156">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourth_Crusade">Fourth Crusade</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1820" data-file-width="2223" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg/250px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg/375px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg/500px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Ferdinand_Victor_Delacroix_012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Entry_of_the_Crusaders_in_Constantinople" title="Entry of the Crusaders in Constantinople">The Entry of the Crusaders into Constantinople</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Eugène Delacroix</a> (1840)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1198, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Pope Innocent III</a> broached the subject of a new crusade through <a href="/wiki/Papal_legate" title="Papal legate">legates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Encyclical" title="Encyclical">encyclical</a> letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich299_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich299-157">[154]</a></sup> The stated intent of the crusade was to conquer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Arab_Egypt#Ayyubid_period" title="History of Arab Egypt">Egypt</a>, the centre of Muslim power in the Levant. The crusader army arrived at <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a> in the summer of 1202 and hired the Venetian fleet to transport them to Egypt. As a payment to the Venetians, they captured the (Christian) port of <a href="/wiki/Zadar#History" title="Zadar">Zara</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatia</a> (vassal city of Venice, which had rebelled and placed itself under Hungary's protection in 1186).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrC_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrC-158">[155]</a></sup> Shortly afterwards, <a href="/wiki/Alexios_IV_Angelos" title="Alexios IV Angelos">Alexios IV Angelos</a>, son of the deposed and blinded Emperor Isaac II, made contacts with the crusaders. Alexios offered to reunite the Byzantine church with Rome, pay the crusaders 200,000 silver marks, join the crusade, and provide all the supplies they needed to reach Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norwich301_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich301-159">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Crusader_sack_of_Constantinople_.281204.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Crusader_sack_of_Constantinople_(1204)">Crusader sack of Constantinople (1204)</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(1203)" title="Siege of Constantinople (1203)">Siege of Constantinople (1203)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(1204)" title="Siege of Constantinople (1204)">Siege of Constantinople (1204)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LatinEmpire2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="902" data-file-width="1715" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/LatinEmpire2.png/250px-LatinEmpire2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/LatinEmpire2.png/375px-LatinEmpire2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/LatinEmpire2.png/500px-LatinEmpire2.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LatinEmpire2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The partition of the empire following the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a>, c. 1204</div></div></div>
<p>The crusaders arrived at Constantinople in the summer of 1203 and quickly attacked, starting a major fire that damaged large parts of the city, and briefly seized control. Alexios III fled from the capital, and Alexios Angelos was elevated to the throne as Alexios IV along with his blind father Isaac. Alexios IV and Isaac II were unable to keep their promises and were deposed by <a href="/wiki/Alexios_V_Doukas" title="Alexios V Doukas">Alexios V</a>. The crusaders again took the city on 13 April 1204, and Constantinople was subjected to pillage and massacre by the rank and file for three days. Many priceless icons, relics and other objects later turned up in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, a large number in Venice. According to chronicler <a href="/wiki/Niketas_Choniates" title="Niketas Choniates">Niketas Choniates</a>, a prostitute was even set up on the patriarchal throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NC_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-NC-160">[157]</a></sup> When order had been restored, the crusaders and the Venetians proceeded to implement their agreement; <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_I,_Latin_Emperor" title="Baldwin I, Latin Emperor">Baldwin of Flanders</a> was elected emperor of a new <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a>, and the Venetian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Morosini" title="Thomas Morosini">Thomas Morosini</a> was chosen as patriarch. The lands divided up among the leaders included most of the former Byzantine possessions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br40 million_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Br40_million-161">[158]</a></sup> Although Venice was more interested in commerce than conquering territory, it took key areas of Constantinople, and the <a href="/wiki/Doge" title="Doge">Doge</a> took the title of "<i>Lord of a Quarter and Half a Quarter of the Roman Empire</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Empire_in_exile">Empire in exile</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Frankokratia" title="Frankokratia">Frankokratia</a></div>
<p>After the sack of Constantinople in 1204 by Latin crusaders, two Byzantine successor states were established: the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Nicaea" title="Empire of Nicaea">Empire of Nicaea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Despotate_of_Epirus" title="Despotate of Epirus">Despotate of Epirus</a>.
