A third, the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond" title="Empire of Trebizond">Empire of Trebizond</a>, was created after <a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_of_Trebizond" title="Alexios I of Trebizond">Alexios</a> <a href="/wiki/Komnenos" title="Komnenos">Komnenos</a>, commanding the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Georgia" title="Kingdom of Georgia">Georgian</a> expedition in <a href="/wiki/Chaldia" title="Chaldia">Chaldia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[160]</a></sup> a few weeks before the sack of Constantinople, found himself <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a> emperor and established himself in Trebizond. Of the three successor states, Epirus and Nicaea stood the best chance of reclaiming Constantinople. The Nicaean Empire struggled to survive the next few decades, however, and by the mid-13th century it had lost much of southern Anatolia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[161]</a></sup> The weakening of the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Rum" title="Sultanate of Rum">Sultanate of Rûm</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_K%C3%B6se_Da%C4%9F" title="Battle of Köse Dağ">Mongol invasion in 1242–1243</a> allowed many <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_beyliks" title="Anatolian beyliks">beyliks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ghazi_(warrior)" title="Ghazi (warrior)">ghazis</a> to set up their own principalities in Anatolia, weakening the Byzantine hold on Asia Minor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[162]</a></sup> In time, one of the Beys, <a href="/wiki/Osman_I" title="Osman I">Osman I</a>, created the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> that would eventually conquer Constantinople. However, the Mongol invasion also gave Nicaea a temporary respite from Seljuk attacks, allowing it to concentrate on the Latin Empire to its north.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reconquest_of_Constantinople">Reconquest of Constantinople</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Palaiologos_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Palaiologos dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Palaiologos dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="925" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg/300px-1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg/450px-1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg/600px-1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1263_Mediterranean_Sea.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Byzantine Empire, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1263</span></div></div></div>
<p>The Empire of Nicaea, founded by the <a href="/wiki/Laskaris" title="Laskaris">Laskarid dynasty</a>, managed to effect the <a href="/wiki/Reconquest_of_Constantinople" title="Reconquest of Constantinople">recapture of Constantinople</a> from the Latins in 1261 and defeat <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a>. This led to a short-lived revival of Byzantine fortunes under <a href="/wiki/Michael_VIII_Palaiologos" title="Michael VIII Palaiologos">Michael VIII Palaiologos</a>, but the war-ravaged empire was ill-equipped to deal with the enemies that surrounded it. To maintain his campaigns against the Latins, Michael pulled troops from Asia Minor and levied crippling taxes on the peasantry, causing much resentment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[163]</a></sup> Massive construction projects were completed in Constantinople to repair the damage of the Fourth Crusade, but none of these initiatives was of any comfort to the farmers in Asia Minor suffering raids from Muslim ghazis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-{{Harvnb|reinert|2002|p=257}}._167-0"><a href="#cite_note-{{Harvnb|reinert|2002|p=257}}.-167">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rather than holding on to his possessions in Asia Minor, Michael chose to expand the empire, gaining only short-term success. To avoid another sacking of the capital by the Latins, he forced the Church to submit to Rome, again a temporary solution for which the peasantry hated Michael and Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-{{Harvnb|reinert|2002|p=257}}._167-1"><a href="#cite_note-{{Harvnb|reinert|2002|p=257}}.-167">[164]</a></sup> The efforts of <a href="/wiki/Andronikos_II_Palaiologos" title="Andronikos II Palaiologos">Andronikos II</a> and later his grandson <a href="/wiki/Andronikos_III_Palaiologos" title="Andronikos III Palaiologos">Andronikos III</a> marked Byzantium's last genuine attempts in restoring the glory of the empire. However, the use of mercenaries by Andronikos II often backfired, with the <a href="/wiki/Catalan_Company" title="Catalan Company">Catalan Company</a> ravaging the countryside and increasing resentment towards Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall">Fall</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_the_Ottomans_and_fall_of_Constantinople">Rise of the Ottomans and fall of Constantinople</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Ottoman_wars" title="Byzantine–Ottoman wars">Byzantine–Ottoman wars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Constantinople_1453.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="365" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Constantinople_1453.jpg/170px-Constantinople_1453.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Constantinople_1453.jpg/255px-Constantinople_1453.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Constantinople_1453.jpg/340px-Constantinople_1453.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Constantinople_1453.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">siege of Constantinople</a> in 1453, depicted in a 15th-century French miniature</div></div></div>
<p>The situation became worse for Byzantium during the civil wars after Andronikos III died. A <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_civil_war_of_1341%E2%80%931347" title="Byzantine civil war of 1341–1347">six-year-long civil war</a> devastated the empire, allowing the Serbian ruler <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Du%C5%A1an" title="Stefan Dušan">Stefan Dušan</a> to overrun most of the empire's remaining territory and establish a <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Empire" title="Serbian Empire">Serbian Empire</a>. In 1354, an earthquake at <a href="/wiki/Gelibolu" title="Gelibolu">Gallipoli</a> devastated the fort, allowing the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Ottomans</a> (who were hired as mercenaries during the civil war by <a href="/wiki/John_VI_Kantakouzenos" title="John VI Kantakouzenos">John VI Kantakouzenos</a>) to establish themselves in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[167]</a></sup> By the time the Byzantine civil wars had ended, the Ottomans had defeated the Serbians and subjugated them as vassals. Following the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kosovo" title="Battle of Kosovo">Battle of Kosovo</a>, much of the Balkans became dominated by the Ottomans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Byzantine emperors appealed to the West for help, but the pope would only consider sending aid in return for a reunion of the Eastern Orthodox Church with the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">See of Rome</a>. Church unity was considered and occasionally accomplished by imperial decree, but the Orthodox citizenry and clergy intensely resented the authority of Rome and the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Rite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[169]</a></sup> Some Western troops arrived to bolster the Christian defence of Constantinople, but most Western rulers, distracted by their own affairs, did nothing as the Ottomans picked apart the remaining Byzantine territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R84-85_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-R84-85-173">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Constantinople by this stage was underpopulated and dilapidated. The population of the city had collapsed so severely that it was now little more than a cluster of villages separated by fields. On 2 April 1453, <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_II" title="Mehmed II">Sultan Mehmed</a>'s army of 80,000 men and large numbers of irregulars laid siege to the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R84-86_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-R84-86-174">[171]</a></sup> Despite a desperate last-ditch defence of the city by the massively outnumbered Christian forces (c. 7,000 men, 2,000 of whom were foreign),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R84-85_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-R84-85-173">[170]</a></sup> Constantinople finally fell to the Ottomans after a two-month siege on 29 May 1453. The final Byzantine emperor, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_XI_Palaiologos" title="Constantine XI Palaiologos">Constantine XI Palaiologos</a>, was last seen casting off his imperial regalia and throwing himself into hand-to-hand combat after the walls of the city were taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_aftermath">Political aftermath</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2369" data-file-width="2497" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg/200px-Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg/300px-Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg/400px-Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Mediterranean_1450.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Eastern Mediterranean just before the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_imperial_flag,_14th_century.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="648" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg/200px-Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg/300px-Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg/400px-Byzantine_imperial_flag%2C_14th_century.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_imperial_flag,_14th_century.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Byzantine_flags_and_insignia" title="Byzantine flags and insignia">Flag of the late Empire</a> under the Palaiologoi, sporting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetragrammic_cross" title="Tetragrammic cross">tetragrammic cross</a> symbol of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaiologos_dynasty" title="Palaiologos dynasty">Palaiologos dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<p>By the time of the fall of Constantinople, the Byzantine Empire, already an empire in name only since the Fourth Crusade, had been reduced to three <a href="/wiki/Rump_state" title="Rump state">rump states</a>, which were the <a href="/wiki/Despotate_of_the_Morea" title="Despotate of the Morea">Despotate of the Morea</a>, the Empire of Trebizond and the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Theodoro" title="Principality of Theodoro">Principality of Theodoro</a>. The Morea was ruled by the brothers of Constantine XI, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Palaiologos" title="Thomas Palaiologos">Thomas Palaiologos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demetrios_Palaiologos" title="Demetrios Palaiologos">Demetrios Palaiologos</a>. The despotate continued as an independent state by paying an annual tribute to the Ottomans. Incompetent rule, failure to pay the annual tribute, and a revolt against the Ottomans finally led to Mehmed II's invasion of Morea in May 1460.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>A few holdouts remained for a time. The island of <a href="/wiki/Monemvasia" title="Monemvasia">Monemvasia</a> refused to surrender, and it was ruled for a short time by an Aragonese corsair. When the population drove him out they obtained the consent of Thomas to place themselves under the pope's protection before the end of 1460. The <a href="/wiki/Mani_Peninsula" title="Mani Peninsula">Mani Peninsula</a>, on the Morea's south end, resisted under a loose coalition of the local clans and then that area came under Venice's rule. The last holdout was Salmeniko, in the Morea's northwest. <a href="/wiki/Graitzas_Palaiologos" title="Graitzas Palaiologos">Graitzas Palaiologos</a> was the military commander there, stationed at <a href="/wiki/Salmeniko_Castle" title="Salmeniko Castle">Salmeniko Castle</a>. While the town eventually surrendered, Graitzas and his garrison and some town residents held out in the castle until July 1461, when they escaped and reached Venetian territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Empire of Trebizond, which had split away from the Byzantine Empire just weeks before Constantinople was taken by the Crusaders in 1204, became the last remnant and last de facto successor state to the Byzantine Empire. Efforts by Emperor <a href="/wiki/David_of_Trebizond" title="David of Trebizond">David</a> to recruit European powers for an anti-Ottoman crusade provoked war between the Ottomans and Trebizond in the summer of 1461. After a <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Trebizond_(1461)" title="Siege of Trebizond (1461)">month-long siege</a>, David surrendered the city of Trebizond on 14 August 1461. Trebizond's Crimean principality, the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Theodoro" title="Principality of Theodoro">Principality of Theodoro</a> (part of the <a href="/wiki/Perateia" title="Perateia">Perateia</a>), lasted another 14 years, falling to the Ottomans in December 1475. Therefore, the last remnant of the Roman Empire had officially ceased to exist, after 2,228 years of Roman civilization, since the legendary <a href="/wiki/Founding_of_Rome" title="Founding of Rome">Founding of Rome</a> in 753 BC.
</p><p>A nephew of the last emperor, Constantine XI, <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Palaiologos" title="Andreas Palaiologos">Andreas Palaiologos</a> claimed to have inherited the title of Byzantine emperor. He lived in the Morea until its fall in 1460, then escaped to Rome where he lived under the protection of the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a> for the remainder of his life. Since the office of the emperor had never been technically hereditary, Andreas' claim would have been without merit under Byzantine law. However, the empire had vanished, and Western states generally followed the Roman-church-sanctioned principles of hereditary sovereignty. Seeking a life in the west, Andreas styled himself <i>Imperator Constantinopolitanus</i> ("Emperor of Constantinople"), and sold his succession rights to both <a href="/wiki/Charles_VIII_of_France" title="Charles VIII of France">Charles VIII of France</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs_of_Spain" title="Catholic Monarchs of Spain">Catholic Monarchs</a>.
</p><p>Constantine XI died without producing an heir, and had Constantinople not fallen he might have been succeeded by the sons of his deceased elder brother, who were taken into the palace service of Mehmed II after the fall of Constantinople. The oldest boy, renamed <a href="/wiki/Hass_Murad_Pasha" title="Hass Murad Pasha">Has Murad</a>, became a personal favorite of Mehmed and served as <a href="/wiki/Beylerbey" title="Beylerbey">beylerbey</a> (governor-general) of the Balkans. The younger son, renamed <a href="/wiki/Mesih_Pasha" title="Mesih Pasha">Mesih Pasha</a>, became admiral of the Ottoman fleet and <a href="/wiki/Sanjak-bey" title="Sanjak-bey">sanjak-bey</a> (governor) of the Province of <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli" title="Gallipoli">Gallipoli</a>. He eventually served twice as <a href="/wiki/Grand_vizier" title="Grand vizier">grand vizier</a> under Mehmed's son, <a href="/wiki/Bayezid_II" title="Bayezid II">Bayezid II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mehmed II and his successors continued to <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_claim_to_Roman_succession" title="Ottoman claim to Roman succession">consider themselves heirs</a> to the Roman Empire until <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Dissolution of the Ottoman Empire">the demise of the Ottoman Empire</a> in the early 20th century following <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. They considered that they had simply shifted their religious basis as Constantine had done before, and they continued to refer to their conquered Eastern Roman inhabitants (<a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy#Christiannity" title="Orthodoxy">Orthodox Christians</a>) as <a href="/wiki/Rum_(endonym)" title="Rum (endonym)">Rûm</a>. Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Danubian_Principalities" title="Danubian Principalities">Danubian Principalities</a> (whose rulers also considered themselves the heirs of the Eastern Roman Emperors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[176]</a></sup>) harbored Orthodox refugees, including some Byzantine nobles.
</p><p>At Constantine's death, the role of the emperor as a patron of Eastern Orthodoxy was claimed by <a href="/wiki/Ivan_III_of_Russia" title="Ivan III of Russia">Ivan III</a>, Grand Prince of <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Muscovy</a>. He had married Andreas' sister, <a href="/wiki/Sophia_Palaiologina" title="Sophia Palaiologina">Sophia Palaiologina</a>, whose grandson, <a href="/wiki/Ivan_the_Terrible" title="Ivan the Terrible">Ivan IV</a>, would become the first tsar of Russia (<i>tsar</i>, or <i>czar</i>, meaning <i>caesar</i>, is a term traditionally applied by Slavs to the Byzantine emperors). Their successors supported the idea that Moscow was the proper heir to Rome and Constantinople. The idea of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> as the successive <a href="/wiki/Succession_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Succession of the Roman Empire">Third Rome</a> was kept alive until its demise with the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_bureaucracy">Government and bureaucracy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_bureaucracy" title="Byzantine bureaucracy">Byzantine bureaucracy</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg"><img alt="Map of Byzantine Empire showing the themes in circa 750" data-file-height="457" data-file-width="743" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg/220px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg/330px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg/440px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-750-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The <i>themes</i>, c. 750</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg"><img alt="Map of Byzantine Empire showing the themes in circa 950" data-file-height="455" data-file-width="747" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg/220px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg/330px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg/440px-Byzantine_Empire_Themata-950-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The <i>themes</i>, c. 950</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>As established by the Hellenistic political systems, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_emperors" title="List of Byzantine emperors">monarch</a> was the sole and <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">absolute ruler</a>, and his power was regarded as having divine origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[178]</a></sup> From Justinian I on, the emperor was considered <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomos_empsychos" title="Nomos empsychos">nomos empsychos</a></i>, the "living law", both lawgiver and administrator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[179]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_senate" title="Byzantine senate">senate</a> had ceased to have real political and legislative authority but remained as an honorary council with titular members, resembling an emergency or ceremonial meeting made up of powerful Constantinopolitan aristocrats, very often friends and relatives of the emperor. By the end of the 8th century, a civil administration focused on the court was formed as part of a large-scale consolidation of power in the capital (the rise to pre-eminence of the position of <i><a href="/wiki/Sakellarios" title="Sakellarios">sakellarios</a></i> is related to this change).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L291_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-L291-183">[180]</a></sup> As result of the different Orthodox and <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Hellenistic political systems</a> philosophies, from Heraclius onwards, an administrative simplification was given way for the emperor's easier management of the state as the sole administrator and lawgiver of the sacred <a href="/wiki/Ecumene" title="Ecumene">Oikoumene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[181]</a></sup> Definitive powers began to be centered around single entities who acted as <a href="/wiki/Viceroy" title="Viceroy">viceroys</a>, starting with the <a href="/wiki/Exarch" title="Exarch">exarchs</a>, who shared the extraordinary powers of the emperor in their respective districts and only answered to him, they also being appointed by the emperor. The most important administrative reform, which probably started in the mid-7th century, was the creation of <a href="/wiki/Theme_(Byzantine_district)" title="Theme (Byzantine district)">themes</a>, where civil and military administration were combined and embodied in a single person, the <i><a href="/wiki/Strategos" title="Strategos">strategos</a>,</i> who also acted as the emperor's viceroy and shared his extraordinary powers in their respective "<i>thémata</i>", they too being also appointed by the emperor directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M60_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-M60-185">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the occasionally derogatory use of the terms "Byzantine" and "<a href="/wiki/Byzantinism" title="Byzantinism">Byzantinism</a>", the Byzantine bureaucracy had a distinct ability for adapting to the empire's changing situations. The elaborate system of titulature and precedence gave the court prestige and influence. Officials were arranged in strict order around the emperor and depended upon the royal will for their ranks. There were also actual administrative jobs, but authority could be vested in individuals rather than offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L34_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-L34-187">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 8th and 9th centuries, civil service had constituted the clearest path to aristocratic status. However, beginning from the 9th century, the civil aristocracy was rivalled by an aristocracy of nobility. According to some studies of the Byzantine government, 11th-century politics were dominated by competition between the civil and the military aristocracy. During this period, Alexios I undertook important administrative reforms, including the creation of new courtly dignities and offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L13_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-L13-188">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diplomacy">Diplomacy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_diplomacy" title="Byzantine diplomacy">Byzantine diplomacy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Foreign relations of the Byzantine Empire">Foreign relations of the Byzantine Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="907" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg/250px-John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg/375px-John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg/500px-John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_the_Grammarian_as_ambassador_before_Theophilos_and_Mamun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The embassy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_John_VII_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch John VII of Constantinople">John the Grammarian</a> in 829, between the emperor <a href="/wiki/Theophilos_(emperor)" title="Theophilos (emperor)">Theophilos</a> and the Abbasid caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">Al-Ma'mun</a></div></div></div>
<p>After the fall of Rome, the key challenge to the empire was maintaining relations with its neighbours. When these nations set about forging formal political institutions, they were often modelled after Constantinople. Byzantine diplomacy managed to draw its neighbours into a network of international and inter-state relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-N1_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-N1-189">[186]</a></sup> This network revolved around treaty-making and included the welcoming of the new ruler into the family of kings and the assimilation of Byzantine social attitudes, values and institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[187]</a></sup> Whereas classical writers are fond of making ethical and legal distinctions between peace and war, Byzantines regarded diplomacy as a form of war by other means. For example, a Bulgarian threat could be countered by providing money to the Kievan Rus'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ant_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ant-191">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sketch_of_Emperor_John_VIII_Palaeologus.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1298" data-file-width="1097" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Sketch_of_Emperor_John_VIII_Palaeologus.jpeg/180px-Sketch_of_Emperor_John_VIII_Palaeologus.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Sketch_of_Emperor_John_VIII_Palaeologus.jpeg/270px-Sketch_of_Emperor_John_VIII_Palaeologus.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Sketch_of_Emperor_John_VIII_Palaeologus.jpeg/360px-Sketch_of_Emperor_John_VIII_Palaeologus.jpeg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sketch_of_Emperor_John_VIII_Palaeologus.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Italian sketch of Emperor <a href="/wiki/John_VIII_Palaiologos" title="John VIII Palaiologos">John VIII</a> during his visit in <a href="/wiki/Ferrara" title="Ferrara">Ferrara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> in 1438</div></div></div>
<p>Diplomacy was understood to have an intelligence-gathering function on top of its pure political function. The <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Barbarians" title="Bureau of Barbarians">Bureau of Barbarians</a> in Constantinople handled matters of protocol and record-keeping for any issues related to the "<a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarians</a>", and thus had, perhaps, a basic intelligence function itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[189]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._B._Bury" title="J. B. Bury">John B. Bury</a> believes that the office exercised supervision over all foreigners visiting Constantinople, and that they were under the supervision of the <a href="/wiki/Logothetes_tou_dromou" title="Logothetes tou dromou">Logothetes tou dromou</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[190]</a></sup> While on the surface a protocol office—its main duty was to ensure foreign envoys were properly cared for and received sufficient state funds for their maintenance, and it kept all the official translators—it probably had a security function as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>Byzantines availed themselves of several diplomatic practices. For example, embassies to the capital often stayed on for years. A member of other royal houses would routinely be requested to stay on in Constantinople as a potential hostage as well as a useful pawn in case political conditions changed. Another key practice was to overwhelm visitors by sumptuous displays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-N1_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-N1-189">[186]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Dimitri_Obolensky" title="Dimitri Obolensky">Dimitri Obolensky</a>, the preservation of the ancient civilisation in Europe could be accredited to the skill and resourcefulness of Byzantine diplomacy, which remains one of Byzantium's lasting contributions to the history of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O3_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-O3-195">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_law" title="Byzantine law">Byzantine law</a></div>
<p>In 438, the <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Theodosianus" title="Codex Theodosianus">Codex Theodosianus</a></i>, named after Theodosius II, codified Byzantine law. It went into force in the Eastern Roman/Byzantine Empire as well as in the Western Roman Empire. It summarised the laws and gave direction on interpretation. Under the reign of Justinian I it was <a href="/wiki/Tribonian" title="Tribonian">Tribonian</a>, a notable jurist, who supervised the revision of the legal code known today as the <i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Corpus Juris Civilis</a></i>. Justinian's reforms had a clear effect on the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">jurisprudence</a>, with his <i>Corpus</i> becoming the basis for <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Roman_law" title="Medieval Roman law">revived Roman law</a> in the Western world, while Leo III's <i>Ecloga</i> influenced the formation of legal institutions in the Slavic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[193]</a></sup> In the 10th century, Leo VI the Wise achieved the complete codification of the whole of Byzantine law in Greek with the <i><a href="/wiki/Basilika" title="Basilika">Basilika</a></i>, which became the foundation of all subsequent Byzantine law with an influence extending through to modern Balkan legal codes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-97-98_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-97-98-109">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_medicine">Science and medicine</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_science" title="Byzantine science">Byzantine science</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_medicine" title="Byzantine medicine">Byzantine medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_philosophy" title="Byzantine philosophy">Byzantine philosophy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_inventions" title="List of Byzantine inventions">List of Byzantine inventions</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20131203_Istanbul_048.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2976" data-file-width="3968" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/20131203_Istanbul_048.jpg/250px-20131203_Istanbul_048.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/20131203_Istanbul_048.jpg/375px-20131203_Istanbul_048.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/20131203_Istanbul_048.jpg/500px-20131203_Istanbul_048.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20131203_Istanbul_048.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interior of the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, the patriarchal <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a> in Constantinople designed 537 by <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a>, the first compiler of Archimedes' various works. The influence of Archimedes' principles of solid geometry is evident.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Byzantine_science" title="Byzantine science">Byzantine science</a> played an important and crucial role in the <a href="/wiki/Greek_contributions_to_the_Islamic_world" title="Greek contributions to the Islamic world">transmission of classical knowledge</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Islamic world</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Renaissance Italy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobins19938_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobins19938-198">[195]</a></sup> Many of the most distinguished classical scholars held high office in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Constantinople" title="University of Constantinople">Imperial University of Constantinople</a>, sometimes known as the <i>University of the Palace Hall of Magnaura</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Πανδιδακτήριον τῆς Μαγναύρας</span>), was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman</a> educational institution that could trace its corporate origins to AD 425, when Emperor Theodosius II founded the <i>Pandidakterion</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek_language" title="Medieval Greek language">Medieval Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Πανδιδακτήριον</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[197]</a></sup> The Pandidakterion was refounded in 1046<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[198]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Constantine_IX_Monomachos" title="Constantine IX Monomachos">Constantine IX Monomachos</a> who created the Departments of Law (Διδασκαλεῖον τῶν Νόμων) and Philosophy (Γυμνάσιον).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[199]</a></sup> At the time various economic schools, colleges, polytechnics, libraries and fine arts academies also operated in the city of Constantinople. And a few scholars have gone so far as to call the Pandidakterion the first "university" in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loukaki_1997,_1553_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loukaki_1997,_1553-203">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p>The writings of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a> were cultivated and preserved in Byzantium. Therefore, Byzantine science was in every period closely connected with <a href="/wiki/Ancient_philosophy" title="Ancient philosophy">ancient philosophy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[201]</a></sup> In the field of engineering <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a>, the Greek mathematician and architect of the Hagia Sophia, produced the first compilation of <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a>' works c. 530, and it is through this manuscript tradition, kept alive by the school of mathematics and engineering founded c. 850 during the "Byzantine Renaissance" by <a href="/wiki/Leo_the_Mathematician" title="Leo the Mathematician">Leo the Mathematician</a>, that such works are known today (see <a href="/wiki/Archimedes_Palimpsest" title="Archimedes Palimpsest">Archimedes Palimpsest</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexandrian philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Philoponus" title="John Philoponus">John Philoponus</a> was the first to question <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics" title="Aristotelian physics">Aristotelian physics</a>. Unlike Aristotle, who based his physics on verbal argument, Philoponus relied on observation. Philoponus' criticism of Aristotelian principles of physics was an inspiration for <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>'s refutation of Aristotelian physics during the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Revolution" title="Scientific Revolution">Scientific Revolution</a> many centuries later, as Galileo cites Philoponus substantially in his works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindberg162_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-lindberg162-207">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Byzantines pioneered the concept of the hospital as an institution offering medical care and the possibility of a cure for the patients, as a reflection of the ideals of Christian charity, rather than merely a place to die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liquid_fire_granades_Chania.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1590" data-file-width="1881" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Liquid_fire_granades_Chania.jpg/170px-Liquid_fire_granades_Chania.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Liquid_fire_granades_Chania.jpg/255px-Liquid_fire_granades_Chania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Liquid_fire_granades_Chania.jpg/340px-Liquid_fire_granades_Chania.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liquid_fire_granades_Chania.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ceramic grenades that were filled with Greek fire, surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Caltrop" title="Caltrop">caltrops</a>, 10th–12th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Historical_Museum_(Greece)" title="National Historical Museum (Greece)">National Historical Museum</a>, Athens, Greece</div></div></div><p>Greek fire, an incendiary weapon which could even burn on water, is attributed to the Byzantines. It played a crucial role in the empire's victory over the Umayyad Caliphate during the siege of Constantinople (717–718).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[206]</a></sup> The discovery is attributed to <a href="/wiki/Callinicus_of_Heliopolis" title="Callinicus of Heliopolis">Callinicus of Heliopolis</a> from Syria who fled during the Arab conquest of Syria. However, it has also been argued that no single person invented Greek fire, but rather, that it was "invented by the chemists in Constantinople who had inherited the discoveries of the Alexandrian chemical school...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-210">[207]</a></sup>
In the final century of the empire, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematical sciences</a> were taught in Trebizond; medicine attracted the interest of almost all scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TM189_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-TM189-211">[208]</a></sup> The Fall of Constantinople in 1453 fuelled the era later commonly known as the "<a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>". During this period, <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">refugee Byzantine scholars</a> were principally responsible for carrying, in person and writing, ancient Greek grammatical, literary studies, mathematical, and astronomical knowledge to early Renaissance Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[209]</a></sup> They also brought with them classical learning and texts on botany, medicine and zoology, as well as the works of Dioscorides and John Philoponus' criticism of Aristotelian physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindberg162_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-lindberg162-207">[204]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tribonian_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="317" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Tribonian_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg/170px-Tribonian_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Tribonian_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg/255px-Tribonian_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Tribonian_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tribonian_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bas-relief plaque of <a href="/wiki/Tribonian" title="Tribonian">Tribonian</a> in the Chamber of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Argyropoulos_(detail)_Calling_of_the_Apostles.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="513" data-file-width="260" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Argyropoulos_%28detail%29_Calling_of_the_Apostles.JPG/140px-Argyropoulos_%28detail%29_Calling_of_the_Apostles.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Argyropoulos_%28detail%29_Calling_of_the_Apostles.JPG/210px-Argyropoulos_%28detail%29_Calling_of_the_Apostles.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/Argyropoulos_%28detail%29_Calling_of_the_Apostles.JPG 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Argyropoulos_(detail)_Calling_of_the_Apostles.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Many <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">refugee Byzantine scholars</a> fled to North Italy in the 1400s. Here <a href="/wiki/John_Argyropoulos" title="John Argyropoulos">John Argyropoulos</a> (1415–1487), born in Constantinople and who ended his days in north Italy.</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">State church of the Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Rite" title="Byzantine Rite">Byzantine Rite</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_in_Istanbul_(focused_on_the_original_Roman_building).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1323" data-file-width="1940" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Hagia_Sophia_in_Istanbul_%28focused_on_the_original_Roman_building%29.jpg/250px-Hagia_Sophia_in_Istanbul_%28focused_on_the_original_Roman_building%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Hagia_Sophia_in_Istanbul_%28focused_on_the_original_Roman_building%29.jpg/375px-Hagia_Sophia_in_Istanbul_%28focused_on_the_original_Roman_building%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Hagia_Sophia_in_Istanbul_%28focused_on_the_original_Roman_building%29.jpg/500px-Hagia_Sophia_in_Istanbul_%28focused_on_the_original_Roman_building%29.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_in_Istanbul_(focused_on_the_original_Roman_building).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>As a symbol and expression of the universal prestige of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Patriarchate of Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian</a> built the Church of the Holy Wisdom of God, <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, which was completed in the short period of four and a half years (532–537).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fethiye_Museum_9607.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1065" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Fethiye_Museum_9607.jpg/250px-Fethiye_Museum_9607.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Fethiye_Museum_9607.jpg/375px-Fethiye_Museum_9607.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Fethiye_Museum_9607.jpg/500px-Fethiye_Museum_9607.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fethiye_Museum_9607.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mosaic of Jesus in <a href="/wiki/Pammakaristos_Church" title="Pammakaristos Church">Pammakaristos Church</a>, Istanbul</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basilica_of_San_Vitale_-_triumphal_arch_mosaics.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4592" data-file-width="3448" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basilica_of_San_Vitale_-_triumphal_arch_mosaics.jpg/200px-Basilica_of_San_Vitale_-_triumphal_arch_mosaics.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basilica_of_San_Vitale_-_triumphal_arch_mosaics.jpg/300px-Basilica_of_San_Vitale_-_triumphal_arch_mosaics.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basilica_of_San_Vitale_-_triumphal_arch_mosaics.jpg/400px-Basilica_of_San_Vitale_-_triumphal_arch_mosaics.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basilica_of_San_Vitale_-_triumphal_arch_mosaics.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Triumphal arch mosaics of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> and the Apostles. In <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_San_Vitale" title="Basilica of San Vitale">Basilica of San Vitale</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>, Italy.</div></div></div>
<p>The Byzantine Empire was a <a href="/wiki/Theocracy" title="Theocracy">theocracy</a>, said to be ruled by <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">God</a> working through the emperor. Jennifer Fretland VanVoorst argues, "The Byzantine Empire became a theocracy in the sense that Christian values and ideals were the foundation of the empire's political ideals and heavily entwined with its political goals."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[210]</a></sup> Steven Runciman says in his book:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The constitution of the Byzantine Empire was based on the conviction that it was the earthly copy of the Kingdom of Heaven. Just as God ruled in Heaven, so the Emperor, made in his image, should rule on earth and carry out his commandments ... It saw itself as a universal empire. Ideally, it should embrace all the peoples of the Earth who, ideally, should all be members of the one true Christian Church, its own Orthodox Church. Just as man was made in God's image, so man's kingdom on Earth was made in the image of the Kingdom of Heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[211]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The survival of the empire in the East assured an active role of the emperor in the affairs of the Church. The Byzantine state inherited from pagan times the administrative and financial routine of administering religious affairs, and this routine was applied to the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">Christian Church</a>. Following the pattern set by <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a> of Caesarea, the Byzantines viewed the emperor as a representative or messenger of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Christ</a>, responsible particularly for the propagation of Christianity among pagans, and for the "externals" of the religion, such as administration and finances. As <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Mango" title="Cyril Mango">Cyril Mango</a> points out, the Byzantine political thinking can be summarised in the motto "One God, one empire, one religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M108_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-M108-215">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>Constantinople is generally considered the "cradle of Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Role_of_Christianity_in_civilization" title="Role of Christianity in civilization">Christian civilization</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[213]</a></sup> The imperial role in the affairs of the Church never developed into a fixed, legally defined system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M14_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-M14-217">[214]</a></sup> Additionally, the decline of Rome and the internal dissension in the other Eastern patriarchates, made the Church of Constantinople, between the 6th and 11th centuries, the richest and most influential centre of <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M19_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-M19-218">[215]</a></sup> Even when the empire was reduced to only a shadow of its former self, the Church continued to exercise significant influence both inside and outside of the imperial frontiers. As <a href="/wiki/George_Ostrogorsky" title="George Ostrogorsky">George Ostrogorsky</a> points out:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Patriarchate of Constantinople</a> remained the centre of the Orthodox world, with subordinate <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_bishop" title="Metropolitan bishop">metropolitan sees</a> and archbishoprics in the territory of Asia Minor and the Balkans, now lost to Byzantium, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, Russia and Lithuania. The Church remained the most stable element in the Byzantine Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M130_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-M130-219">[216]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Byzantine monasticism especially came to be an "ever-present feature" of the empire, with monasteries becoming "powerful landowners and a voice to be listened to in imperial politics".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[217]</a></sup>
</p><p>The official state Christian doctrine was determined by the <a href="/wiki/First_seven_ecumenical_councils" title="First seven ecumenical councils">first seven ecumenical councils</a>, and it was then the emperor's duty to impose it on his subjects. An imperial decree of 388, which was later incorporated into the <i>Codex Justinianeus</i>, orders the population of the empire "to assume the name of Catholic Christians", and regards all those who will not abide by the law as "mad and foolish persons"; as followers of "heretical dogmas".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite imperial decrees and the stringent stance of the <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">state church</a>, which came to be known as the Eastern Orthodox Church or <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a>, the Eastern Orthodox Church never represented all Christians in Byzantium. Mango believes that in the early stages of the empire, the "mad and foolish persons"—those labelled "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heretics" title="Heretics">heretics</a>" by the state church—were the majority of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M109_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-M109-222">[219]</a></sup> Besides the <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagans</a> who existed until the end of the 6th century, and the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="History of the Jews in the Byzantine Empire">Jews</a>, there were many followers—sometimes even emperors—of various Christian doctrines, such as <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monophysitism" title="Monophysitism">Monophysitism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arianism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paulicianism" title="Paulicianism">Paulicianism</a>, whose teachings were in some opposition to the main theological doctrine as determined by the Ecumenical Councils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[220]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another division among Christians occurred, when Leo III ordered the destruction of icons throughout the Empire. This led to a significant religious crisis, which ended in the mid-9th century with the restoration of icons. During the same period, a new wave of pagans emerged in the Balkans, originating mainly from Slavic people. These were gradually <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianised</a>, and by Byzantium's late stages, Eastern Orthodoxy represented most Christians and, in general, most people in what remained of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M115_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-M115-224">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jews were a significant minority in the Byzantine state throughout its history, and according to Roman law, they constituted a legally recognised religious group. In the early Byzantine period they were generally tolerated, but then periods of tensions and persecutions ensued. In any case, after the Arab conquests the majority of Jews found themselves outside the empire; those left inside the Byzantine borders apparently lived in relative peace from the 10th century onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M114_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-M114-225">[222]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_arts">The arts</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_dress" title="Byzantine dress">Byzantine dress</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:281px;max-width:281px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:139px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aya_Sophia_(7144824757).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1225" data-file-width="2449" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Aya_Sophia_%287144824757%29.jpg/279px-Aya_Sophia_%287144824757%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Aya_Sophia_%287144824757%29.jpg/419px-Aya_Sophia_%287144824757%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Aya_Sophia_%287144824757%29.jpg/558px-Aya_Sophia_%287144824757%29.jpg 2x" width="279"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:107px;max-width:107px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:139px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg/105px-Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg/158px-Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg/210px-Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg 2x" width="105"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:203px;max-width:203px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:117px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2429" data-file-width="4176" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/201px-Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/302px-Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/402px-Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 2x" width="201"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:185px;max-width:185px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:117px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2007" data-file-width="3132" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/183px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/275px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/366px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg 2x" width="183"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, Turkey), 537, by <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_of_Tralles" title="Anthemius of Tralles">Anthemius of Tralles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>Surviving <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine art</a> is mostly religious and with exceptions at certain periods is highly conventionalised, following traditional models that translate carefully controlled church theology into artistic terms. Painting in <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a> and on wood panel and, especially in earlier periods, mosaic were the main media, and figurative <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculpture</a> very rare except for small <a href="/wiki/Ivory_carving" title="Ivory carving">carved ivories</a>. Manuscript painting preserved to the end some of the classical realist tradition that was missing in larger works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[223]</a></sup> Byzantine art was highly prestigious and sought-after in Western Europe, where it maintained a continuous influence on <a href="/wiki/Medieval_art" title="Medieval art">medieval art</a> until near the end of the period. This was especially so in Italy, where Byzantine styles persisted in modified form through the 12th century, and became formative influences on <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a> art. But few incoming influences affected the Byzantine style. With the expansion of the Eastern Orthodox Church, Byzantine forms and styles spread throughout the Orthodox world and beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>Influences from <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine architecture</a>, particularly in religious buildings, can be found in diverse regions from Egypt and Arabia to <a href="/wiki/Neo-Byzantine_architecture_in_the_Russian_Empire" title="Neo-Byzantine architecture in the Russian Empire">Russia</a> and Romania. Byzantine architecture is known for the use of <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">domes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pendentive" title="Pendentive">pendentive</a> architecture was invented in the Byzantine Empire. It also often featured marble columns, <a href="/wiki/Coffer" title="Coffer">coffered</a> ceilings and sumptuous decoration, including the extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaics</a> with golden backgrounds. The building material used by Byzantine architects was no longer marble, which was very appreciated by the Ancient Greeks. They used mostly stone and brick, and also thin <a href="/wiki/Alabaster" title="Alabaster">alabaster</a> sheets for windows. Mosaics were used to cover brick walls, and any other surface where fresco wouldn't resist. Good examples of mosaics from the proto-Byzantine era are in <a href="/wiki/Hagios_Demetrios" title="Hagios Demetrios">Hagios Demetrios</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a> (Greece), the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Sant%27Apollinare_Nuovo" title="Basilica of Sant'Apollinare Nuovo">Basilica of Sant'Apollinare Nuovo</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_San_Vitale" title="Basilica of San Vitale">Basilica of San Vitale</a>, both in Ravenna (Italy), and Hagia Sophia in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>. Greco-Roman temples and Byzantine churches differ substantially in terms of their exterior and interior aspect. In Antiquity, the exterior was the most important part of the temple, because in the interior, where the cult statue of the deity to whom the temple was built was kept, only the priest had access. The ceremonies here held outside, and what the worshipers view was the facade of the temple, consisting of columns, with an <a href="/wiki/Entablature" title="Entablature">entablature</a> and two pediments. Meanwhile, Christian liturgies were held in the interior of the churches, the exterior usually having little to no ornamentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[226]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:42.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%22The_good_Shepherd%22_mosaic_-_Mausoleum_of_Galla_Placidia.jpg" title="Christ as the Good Shepherd; c. 425–430; mosaic; width: c. 3 m; Mausoleum of Galla Placidia (Ravenna, Italy)[227]"><img alt="Christ as the Good Shepherd; c. 425–430; mosaic; width: c. 3 m; Mausoleum of Galla Placidia (Ravenna, Italy)[227]" data-file-height="2645" data-file-width="4179" decoding="async" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/%22The_good_Shepherd%22_mosaic_-_Mausoleum_of_Galla_Placidia.jpg/150px-%22The_good_Shepherd%22_mosaic_-_Mausoleum_of_Galla_Placidia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/%22The_good_Shepherd%22_mosaic_-_Mausoleum_of_Galla_Placidia.jpg/225px-%22The_good_Shepherd%22_mosaic_-_Mausoleum_of_Galla_Placidia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/%22The_good_Shepherd%22_mosaic_-_Mausoleum_of_Galla_Placidia.jpg/300px-%22The_good_Shepherd%22_mosaic_-_Mausoleum_of_Galla_Placidia.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KHM_Wien_Kaiserin_Ariadne_X_39.jpg" title="Diptych Leaf with a Byzantine Empress; 6th century; ivory with traces of gilding and leaf; height: 26.5 cm (10.4 in); Kunsthistorisches Museum (Vienna, Austria)[228]"><img alt="Diptych Leaf with a Byzantine Empress; 6th century; ivory with traces of gilding and leaf; height: 26.5 cm (10.4 in); Kunsthistorisches Museum (Vienna, Austria)[228]" data-file-height="3012" data-file-width="1566" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/KHM_Wien_Kaiserin_Ariadne_X_39.jpg/78px-KHM_Wien_Kaiserin_Ariadne_X_39.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/KHM_Wien_Kaiserin_Ariadne_X_39.jpg/117px-KHM_Wien_Kaiserin_Ariadne_X_39.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/KHM_Wien_Kaiserin_Ariadne_X_39.jpg/156px-KHM_Wien_Kaiserin_Ariadne_X_39.jpg 2x" width="78"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Diptych Leaf with a Byzantine Empress; 6th century; ivory with traces of gilding and leaf; height: 26.5 cm (10.4 in); <a href="/wiki/Kunsthistorisches_Museum" title="Kunsthistorisches Museum">Kunsthistorisches Museum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, Austria)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[228]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:35.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Officina_costantinopolitana,_tesoro_di_asyut_(egitto),_V-VI_sec_ca._01_collier.JPG" title="Collier; late 6th–7th century; gold, an emerald, a sapphire, amethysts and pearls; diameter: 23 cm (9.1 in); from a Constantinopolitan workshop; Antikensammlung Berlin (Berlin, Germany)[229]"><img alt="Collier; late 6th–7th century; gold, an emerald, a sapphire, amethysts and pearls; diameter: 23 cm (9.1 in); from a Constantinopolitan workshop; Antikensammlung Berlin (Berlin, Germany)[229]" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="2340" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Officina_costantinopolitana%2C_tesoro_di_asyut_%28egitto%29%2C_V-VI_sec_ca._01_collier.JPG/150px-Officina_costantinopolitana%2C_tesoro_di_asyut_%28egitto%29%2C_V-VI_sec_ca._01_collier.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Officina_costantinopolitana%2C_tesoro_di_asyut_%28egitto%29%2C_V-VI_sec_ca._01_collier.JPG/225px-Officina_costantinopolitana%2C_tesoro_di_asyut_%28egitto%29%2C_V-VI_sec_ca._01_collier.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Officina_costantinopolitana%2C_tesoro_di_asyut_%28egitto%29%2C_V-VI_sec_ca._01_collier.JPG/300px-Officina_costantinopolitana%2C_tesoro_di_asyut_%28egitto%29%2C_V-VI_sec_ca._01_collier.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
