</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_literature" title="Byzantine literature">Byzantine literature</a>, three different cultural elements are recognised: the <a href="/wiki/Greek_literature" title="Greek literature">Greek</a>, the Christian, and the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Oriental</a>. Byzantine literature is often classified in five groups: historians and annalists; encyclopaedists (<a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Patriarch Photios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Psellos" title="Michael Psellos">Michael Psellus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Choniates" title="Michael Choniates">Michael Choniates</a> are regarded as the greatest encyclopaedists of Byzantium) and essayists; writers of secular poetry (the only genuine heroic epic of the Byzantines is the <i><a href="/wiki/Digenes_Akritas" title="Digenes Akritas">Digenis Acritas</a>);</i> ecclesiastical and theological literature; and popular poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mango275–276_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mango275–276-233">[230]</a></sup> Of the 2,000 to 3,000 volumes of Byzantine literature that survive, only 330 consist of secular poetry, history, science and pseudo-science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mango275–276_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mango275–276-233">[230]</a></sup> While the most flourishing period of the secular literature of Byzantium runs from the 9th to the 12th century, its religious literature (<a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">sermons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_book" title="Liturgical book">liturgical books</a> and poetry, theology, devotional treatises, etc.) developed much earlier with <a href="/wiki/Romanos_the_Melodist" title="Romanos the Melodist">Romanos the Melodist</a> being its most prominent representative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CLi_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-CLi-234">[231]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_composers" title="List of Byzantine composers">List of Byzantine composers</a></div>
<div class="noresize thumb tright" style=";">
<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:202px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:202px; overflow:hidden; position:relative; background-color:white;">
<div style=";left:-210px; top:-100px; width:800px; position:absolute;"> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg" title='Late 4th century "Mosaic of the Musicians" with organ, aulos, and lyre from a Byzantine villa in Maryamin, Syria[232]'><img alt="" data-file-height="1180" data-file-width="1678" decoding="async" height="563" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/800px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/1200px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/1600px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a></div>
<div style="text-align:left; background-color:transparent; line-height:110%;">
</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1180" data-file-width="1678" decoding="async" height="563" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/800px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/1200px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg/1600px-Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosaic_of_the_Female_Musicians.jpg" title="File:Mosaic of the Female Musicians.jpg"> </a></div>Late 4th century "Mosaic of the Musicians" with <a href="/wiki/Pipe_organ" title="Pipe organ">organ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aulos" title="Aulos">aulos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyre</a> from a Byzantine villa in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maryamin,_Hama" title="Maryamin, Hama">Maryamin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ring_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ring-235">[232]</a></sup></div>
</div></div>
<p>The ecclesiastical forms of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_music" title="Byzantine music">Byzantine music—</a>composed to Greek texts as ceremonial, festival, or church music<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[233]</a></sup>—are today the most well-known forms. Ecclesiastical chants were a fundamental part of this genre. Greek and foreign historians agree that the ecclesiastical tones and in general the whole system of Byzantine music is closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Music_of_ancient_Greece" title="Music of ancient Greece">ancient Greek system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[234]</a></sup> It remains the oldest genre of extant music, of which the manner of performance and (with increasing accuracy from the 5th century onwards) the names of the composers, and sometimes the particulars of each musical work's circumstances, are known.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg/200px-Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg/300px-Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/30/Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Lyra_Museo_Nazionale.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earliest known depiction of a <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_lyra" title="Byzantine lyra">bowed lyra</a>, from a Byzantine ivory casket (900–1100) (<i>Museo Nazionale, Florence</i>)</div></div></div>
<p>The 9th-century Persian geographer <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khordadbeh" title="Ibn Khordadbeh">Ibn Khordadbeh</a>, in his lexicographical discussion of instruments, cited the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_lyra" title="Byzantine lyra">lyra</a> (lūrā) as the typical instrument of the Byzantines along with the <i>urghun</i> (organ), <i>shilyani</i> (probably a type of <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyre</a>) and the <i>salandj</i> (probably a <a href="/wiki/Bagpipes" title="Bagpipes">bagpipe</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kartomi124_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kartomi124-238">[235]</a></sup> The first of these, the early bowed stringed instrument known as the Byzantine lyra, came to be called the <i><a href="/wiki/Lira_da_braccio" title="Lira da braccio">lira da braccio</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[236]</a></sup> in Venice, where it is considered by many to have been the predecessor of the contemporary violin, which later flourished there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arkenberg109_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arkenberg109-240">[237]</a></sup> The bowed "lyra" is still played in former Byzantine regions, where it is known as the <a href="/wiki/Classical_kemen%C3%A7e" title="Classical kemençe">Politiki lyra</a> (<abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'lyra of the City', i.e. Constantinople) in Greece, the <a href="/wiki/Calabrian_lira" title="Calabrian lira">Calabrian lira</a> in southern Italy, and the <a href="/wiki/Lijerica" title="Lijerica">lijerica</a> in Dalmatia. The <a href="/wiki/Water_organ" title="Water organ">water organ</a> originated in the Hellenistic world and was used in the <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome" title="Hippodrome">Hippodrome</a> during races.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bush-Kassel-327_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bush-Kassel-327-242">[239]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Pipe_organ" title="Pipe organ">pipe organ</a> with "great leaden pipes" was sent by Emperor Constantine V to <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin the Short</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Frankish_kings#Kings_of_the_Franks" title="List of Frankish kings">King of the Franks</a> in 757. Pepin's son <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> requested a similar organ for his chapel in <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a> in 812, beginning its establishment in Western church music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bush-Kassel-327_242-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bush-Kassel-327-242">[239]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Aulos" title="Aulos">aulos</a> was a double-reeded woodwind like the modern <a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboe</a> or Armenian <a href="/wiki/Duduk" title="Duduk">duduk</a>. Other forms include the <i>plagiaulos</i> (<i>πλαγίαυλος</i>, from <i>πλάγιος</i> "sideways"), which resembled the <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flute</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howard_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howard-243">[240]</a></sup> and the <i>askaulos</i> (ἀσκός <i>askos</i> – <a href="/wiki/Wineskin" title="Wineskin">wineskin</a>), a bagpipe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google15_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-google15-244">[241]</a></sup> Bagpipes, also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Dankiyo" title="Dankiyo">dankiyo</a></i> (from ancient Greek: angion (Τὸ ἀγγεῖον) "the container"), had been played even in Roman times and continued to be played throughout the empire's former realms through to the present. (See Balkan <a href="/wiki/Gaida" title="Gaida">Gaida</a>, Greek <a href="/wiki/Tsampouna" title="Tsampouna">Tsampouna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greek" title="Pontic Greek">Pontic</a> <a href="/wiki/Tulum_(bagpipe)" title="Tulum (bagpipe)">Tulum</a>, Cretan <a href="/wiki/Askomandoura" title="Askomandoura">Askomandoura</a>, Armenian <a href="/wiki/Parkapzuk" title="Parkapzuk">Parkapzuk</a>, and Romanian <a href="/wiki/Cimpoi" title="Cimpoi">Cimpoi</a>.) The modern descendant of the aulos is the Greek <a href="/wiki/Zurna" title="Zurna">Zourna</a>. Other instruments used in Byzantine Music were <a href="/wiki/Qanun_(instrument)" title="Qanun (instrument)">Kanonaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oud" title="Oud">Oud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laouto" title="Laouto">Laouto</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santouri" title="Santouri">Santouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tambouras" title="Tambouras">Tambouras</a>, Seistron (defi tambourine), <a href="/wiki/Toubeleki" title="Toubeleki">Toubeleki</a> and Daouli. Some claim that <a href="/wiki/Lavta" title="Lavta">Lavta</a> may have been invented by the Byzantines before the arrival of the Turks.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_cuisine" title="Byzantine cuisine">Byzantine cuisine</a></div>
<p>Byzantine culture was initially the same as Late Greco-Roman, but over the following millennium of the empire's existence it slowly changed into something more similar to modern Balkan and Anatolian culture. The cuisine still relied heavily on the Greco-Roman fish-sauce condiment <a href="/wiki/Garum" title="Garum">garos</a>, but it also contained foods still familiar today, such as the cured meat <a href="/wiki/Pastirma" title="Pastirma">pastirma</a> (known as "paston" in Byzantine Greek),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[244]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baklava" title="Baklava">baklava</a> (known as <a href="/wiki/Placenta_cake" title="Placenta cake">koptoplakous</a> κοπτοπλακοῦς),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[245]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tiropita" title="Tiropita">tiropita</a> (known as plakountas tetyromenous or tyritas plakountas),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[246]</a></sup> and the famed medieval sweet wines (<a href="/wiki/Malvasia" title="Malvasia">Malvasia</a> from <a href="/wiki/Monemvasia" title="Monemvasia">Monemvasia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commandaria" title="Commandaria">Commandaria</a> and the eponymous <a href="/wiki/Rumney_wine" title="Rumney wine">Rumney wine</a>). <a href="/wiki/Retsina" title="Retsina">Retsina</a>, wine flavoured with pine resin, was also drunk, as it still is in Greece today, producing similar reactions from unfamiliar visitors; "To add to our calamity the Greek wine, on account of being mixed with pitch, resin, and plaster was to us undrinkable," complained <a href="/wiki/Liutprand_of_Cremona" title="Liutprand of Cremona">Liutprand of Cremona</a>, who was the ambassador sent to Constantinople in 968 by the German Holy Roman Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_I" title="Otto I">Otto I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fordham_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fordham-250">[247]</a></sup> The garos fish sauce condiment was also not much appreciated by the unaccustomed; Liutprand of Cremona described being served food covered in an "exceedingly bad fish liquor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fordham_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fordham-250">[247]</a></sup> The Byzantines also used a soy sauce-like condiment, <a href="/wiki/Murri_(condiment)" title="Murri (condiment)">murri</a>, a fermented barley sauce, which, like soy sauce, provided <a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">umami</a> flavouring to their dishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[249]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flags_and_insignia">Flags and insignia</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_flags_and_insignia" title="Byzantine flags and insignia">Byzantine flags and insignia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Palaiologos_Eagle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="770" data-file-width="630" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Byzantine_Palaiologos_Eagle.svg/200px-Byzantine_Palaiologos_Eagle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Byzantine_Palaiologos_Eagle.svg/300px-Byzantine_Palaiologos_Eagle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Byzantine_Palaiologos_Eagle.svg/400px-Byzantine_Palaiologos_Eagle.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Palaiologos_Eagle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Double-headed_eagle" title="Double-headed eagle">The double-headed eagle</a>, a common Imperial symbol</div></div></div>
<p>For most of its history, the Byzantine Empire did not know or use <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">heraldry</a> in the West European sense. Various emblems (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">σημεία</span>, <i>sēmeia</i>; sing. σημείον, <i>sēmeion</i>) were used in official occasions and for military purposes, such as banners or shields displaying various motifs such as the <a href="/wiki/Cross" title="Cross">cross</a> or the <i><a href="/wiki/Labarum" title="Labarum">labarum</a></i>. The use of the cross and images of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Christ</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">Virgin Mary</a> and various saints is also <a href="/wiki/Sigillography" title="Sigillography">attested on seals</a> of officials, but these were personal rather than family emblems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKazhdan1991472,_999_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKazhdan1991472,_999-253">[250]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">Medieval Greek</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:294px;max-width:294px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mudil_Psalter.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="358" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mudil_Psalter.jpg/140px-Mudil_Psalter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mudil_Psalter.jpg/210px-Mudil_Psalter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mudil_Psalter.jpg/280px-Mudil_Psalter.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:148px;max-width:148px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joshua_Roll.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1365" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Joshua_Roll.jpg/146px-Joshua_Roll.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Joshua_Roll.jpg/219px-Joshua_Roll.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Joshua_Roll.jpg/292px-Joshua_Roll.jpg 2x" width="146"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Left: The Mudil Psalter, the oldest complete <a href="/wiki/Psalter" title="Psalter">psalter</a> in the Coptic language (<a href="/wiki/Coptic_Museum" title="Coptic Museum">Coptic Museum</a>, Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Cairo" title="Coptic Cairo">Coptic Cairo</a>)<br/>
Right: The <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Roll" title="Joshua Roll">Joshua Roll</a>, a 10th-century illuminated Greek manuscript possibly made in Constantinople (<a href="/wiki/Vatican_Library" title="Vatican Library">Vatican Library</a>, Rome)</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_Asia_Minor_dialects.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="212" data-file-width="242" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Greek_Asia_Minor_dialects.png/220px-Greek_Asia_Minor_dialects.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Greek_Asia_Minor_dialects.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_Asia_Minor_dialects.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of Greek dialects in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> in the late Byzantine Empire through to 1923. <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek#Demotic" title="Modern Greek">Demotic</a> in yellow. <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greek" title="Pontic Greek">Pontic</a> in orange. <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Greek" title="Cappadocian Greek">Cappadocian</a> in green. (Green dots indicate Cappadocian Greek-speaking villages in 1910.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[251]</a></sup>)</div></div></div>
<p>Apart from the Imperial court, administration and military, the primary language used in the eastern Roman provinces even before the decline of the Western Empire was Greek, having been spoken in the region for centuries before Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[252]</a></sup> Following Rome's conquest of the east its 'Pax Romana', inclusionist political practices and development of public infrastructure, facilitated the further spreading and entrenchment of the Greek language in the east. Indeed, early on in the life of the Roman Empire, Greek had become the common language of the Church, the language of scholarship and the arts, and to a large degree the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> for trade between provinces and with other nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[253]</a></sup> Greek for a time became <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossic</a> with the spoken language, known as <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine</a> (eventually evolving into <a href="/wiki/Demotic_Greek" title="Demotic Greek">Demotic Greek</a>), used alongside an older written form (<a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">Attic Greek</a>) until <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine</a> won out as the spoken and written standard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[254]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Emperor <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> sought to renew the authority of Latin, making it the official language of the Roman administration also in the East, and the Greek expression ἡ κρατοῦσα διάλεκτος <i>(hē kratousa dialektos)</i> attests to the status of Latin as "the language of power."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[255]</a></sup> In the early 5th century, Greek gained equal status with Latin as the official language in the East, and emperors gradually began to legislate in Greek rather than Latin starting with the reign of Leo I the Thracian in the 460s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theinheritance_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-theinheritance-44">[41]</a></sup> The last Eastern emperor to stress the importance of Latin was Justinian I, whose <i>Corpus Juris Civilis</i> was written almost entirely in Latin. He may also have been the last native Latin-speaking emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theinheritance_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-theinheritance-44">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of Latin as the language of administration persisted for centuries, though it was increasingly replaced by Greek. Scholarly Latin rapidly fell into disuse among the educated classes although the language continued to be at least a ceremonial part of the Empire's culture for some time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ApWr_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-ApWr-259">[256]</a></sup> Additionally, Latin remained a minority language in the empire, mainly on the Italian peninsula, along the Dalmatian coast and in the Balkans (specially in mountainous areas away from the coast), eventually developing into various <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a> like <a href="/wiki/Dalmatian_language" title="Dalmatian language">Dalmatian</a> or <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many other languages existed in the multi-ethnic empire, and some of these were given limited official status in their provinces at various times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris2014_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris2014-261">[258]</a></sup> Notably, by the beginning of the Middle Ages, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> had become more widely used by the educated classes in the far eastern provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BJVe_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-BJVe-262">[259]</a></sup> Similarly <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian</a> became significant among the educated in their provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[260]</a></sup> Later foreign contacts made <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Arabic</a> important in the empire and its sphere of influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[261]</a></sup> There was a revival of Latin studies in the 10th century for the same reason and by the 11th century knowledge of Latin was no longer unusual at Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[262]</a></sup> There was widespread use of the Armenian and various Slavic languages, which became more pronounced in the border regions of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris2014_261-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harris2014-261">[258]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aside from these languages, since Constantinople was a prime trading center in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Mediterranean_region" title="History of the Mediterranean region">Mediterranean region</a> and beyond, virtually every known language of the Middle Ages was spoken in the empire at some time, even <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[263]</a></sup> As the empire entered its final decline, its citizens became more culturally homogeneous and the Greek language became integral to their identity and religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[264]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreation">Recreation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="303" data-file-width="585" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg/220px-Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg/330px-Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg/440px-Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tabula_-_boardgame_-_Zeno_game.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A game of <a href="/wiki/Tabula_(game)" title="Tabula (game)">τάβλι (tabula)</a> played by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> emperor <a href="/wiki/Zeno_(emperor)" title="Zeno (emperor)">Zeno</a> in 480 and recorded by <a href="/wiki/Agathias" title="Agathias">Agathias</a> in c. 530 because of a very unlucky dice throw for Zeno (red), as he threw 2, 5 and 6 and was forced to leave eight pieces alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Austin_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-Austin-268">[265]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Byzantines were avid players of <a href="/wiki/Tabula_(game)" title="Tabula (game)">tavli</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greek" title="Byzantine Greek">Byzantine Greek</a>: τάβλη), a game known in English as <a href="/wiki/Backgammon" title="Backgammon">backgammon</a>, which is still popular in former Byzantine realms and still known by the name tavli in Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Austin_268-1"><a href="#cite_note-Austin-268">[265]</a></sup> Byzantine nobles were devoted to horsemanship, particularly <a href="/wiki/Tzykanisterion" title="Tzykanisterion">tzykanion</a>, now known as <a href="/wiki/Polo" title="Polo">polo</a>. The game came from Sassanid Persia, and a Tzykanisterion (stadium for playing the game) was built by Theodosius II inside the <a href="/wiki/Great_Palace_of_Constantinople" title="Great Palace of Constantinople">Great Palace of Constantinople</a>. Emperor Basil I excelled at it; Emperor Alexander died from exhaustion while playing, Emperor Alexios I Komnenos was injured while playing with <a href="/wiki/Tatikios" title="Tatikios">Tatikios</a>, and John I of Trebizond died from a fatal injury during a game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[267]</a></sup> Aside from Constantinople and Trebizond, other Byzantine cities also featured <i>tzykanisteria</i>, most notably <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, an indication of a thriving urban aristocracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[268]</a></sup> The game was introduced to the West by crusaders, who developed a taste for it particularly during the pro-Western reign of Emperor Manuel I Komnenos. Chariot races were popular and held at hippodromes across the empire. There were initially four major factions in chariot racing, differentiated by the colour of the uniform in which they competed; the colours were also <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_dress" title="Byzantine dress">worn</a> by their supporters. These were the Blues (<i>Veneti</i>), the Greens (<i>Prasini</i>), the Reds (<i>Russati</i>), and the Whites (<i>Albati</i>), although by the Byzantine era the only teams with any influence were the Blues and Greens. Emperor Justinian I was a supporter of the Blues.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women">Women</span></h3>
<p>The position of <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Women in the Byzantine Empire">women in the Byzantine Empire</a> essentially represents the position of women in ancient Rome transformed by the introduction of Christianity, with certain rights and customs being lost and replaced, while others were allowed to remain. There were individual Byzantine women famed for their educational accomplishments. However, the general view of women's education was that it was sufficient for a girl to learn domestic duties and to study the lives of the Christian saints and memorize psalms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guglielmo_Cavallo_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guglielmo_Cavallo-272">[269]</a></sup> and to learn to read so that she could study Bible scriptures—although literacy in women was sometimes discouraged because it was believed it could encourage vice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_Stephenson_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Stephenson-273">[270]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Roman right to divorce was gradually erased after the introduction of Christianity and replaced with legal separation and annulment. Marriage was regarded as the ideal state for a woman, and only convent life was seen as a legitimate alternative. Within marriage, sexual activity was regarded only as a means of reproduction. Women had the right to appear before court, but her testimony was not regarded as equal to that of a man and could be contradicted based on her sex if put against that of a man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guglielmo_Cavallo_272-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guglielmo_Cavallo-272">[269]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the 6th century there was a growing ideal of <a href="/wiki/Sex_segregation" title="Sex segregation">gender segregation</a>, which dictated that women should wear veils<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcus_Louis_Rautman_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marcus_Louis_Rautman-274">[271]</a></sup> and only be seen in public when attending church,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynda_Garland_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lynda_Garland-275">[272]</a></sup> and while the ideal was never fully enforced, it influenced society. The laws of Emperor Justinian I made it legal for a man to divorce his wife for attending public premises such as theatres or public baths without his permission,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonathan_Harris_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan_Harris-276">[273]</a></sup> and Emperor Leo VI banned women from witnessing business contracts with the argument that it caused them to come in contact with men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guglielmo_Cavallo_272-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guglielmo_Cavallo-272">[269]</a></sup>  In Constantinople upper-class women were increasingly expected to keep to a special women's section (<i>gynaikonitis</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynda_Garland_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lynda_Garland-275">[272]</a></sup> and by the 8th century it was described as unacceptable for unmarried daughters to meet unrelated men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guglielmo_Cavallo_272-3"><a href="#cite_note-Guglielmo_Cavallo-272">[269]</a></sup>  While imperial women and their ladies appeared in public alongside men, women and men at the imperial court attended royal banquets separately until the rise of the Komnenos dynasty in the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynda_Garland_275-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lynda_Garland-275">[272]</a></sup>
</p><p>Eastern Roman and later Byzantine women retained the Roman woman's right to inherit, own and manage their property and signs contracts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynda_Garland_275-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lynda_Garland-275">[272]</a></sup> rights which were far superior to the rights of married women in Medieval Catholic Western Europe, as these rights included both married women as well as unmarried women and widows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonathan_Harris_276-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan_Harris-276">[273]</a></sup>  Women's legal right to handle their own money made it possible for rich women to engage in business, however women who actively had to find a profession to support themselves normally worked as domestics or in domestic fields such as the food or textile industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonathan_Harris_276-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan_Harris-276">[273]</a></sup>  Women could work as medical physicians and attendants of women patients and visitors at hospitals and public baths with government support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_Stephenson_273-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_Stephenson-273">[270]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the introduction of Christianity, women could no longer become priestesses, but it became common for women to found and manage nunneries, which functioned as schools for girls as well as asylums, poor houses, hospitals, prisons and retirement homes for women, and many Byzantine women practised social work as lay sisters and deaconesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynda_Garland_275-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lynda_Garland-275">[272]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_silk" title="Byzantine silk">Byzantine silk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">Sino-Roman relations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gold_coin_of_Justinian_I_527CE_565CE_excavated_in_India_probably_in_the_south.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1423" data-file-width="1412" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Gold_coin_of_Justinian_I_527CE_565CE_excavated_in_India_probably_in_the_south.jpg/170px-Gold_coin_of_Justinian_I_527CE_565CE_excavated_in_India_probably_in_the_south.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Gold_coin_of_Justinian_I_527CE_565CE_excavated_in_India_probably_in_the_south.jpg/255px-Gold_coin_of_Justinian_I_527CE_565CE_excavated_in_India_probably_in_the_south.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Gold_coin_of_Justinian_I_527CE_565CE_excavated_in_India_probably_in_the_south.jpg/340px-Gold_coin_of_Justinian_I_527CE_565CE_excavated_in_India_probably_in_the_south.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gold_coin_of_Justinian_I_527CE_565CE_excavated_in_India_probably_in_the_south.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Golden Solidus of <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> (527–565) excavated in India probably in the south, an example of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade" title="Indo-Roman trade">Indo-Roman trade</a> during the period</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_economy" title="Byzantine economy">Byzantine economy</a> was among the most advanced in Europe and the Mediterranean for many centuries. Europe, in particular, could not match Byzantine economic strength until late in the Middle Ages. Constantinople operated as a prime hub in a trading network that at various times extended across nearly all of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, in particular as the primary western terminus of the famous <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>. Until the first half of the 6th century and in sharp contrast with the decaying West, the Byzantine economy was flourishing and resilient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[274]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a> and the Arab conquests represented a substantial reversal of fortunes contributing to a period of stagnation and decline. Isaurian reforms and Constantine V's repopulation, public works and tax measures marked the beginning of a revival that continued until 1204, despite territorial contraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[275]</a></sup> From the 10th century until the end of the 12th, the Byzantine Empire projected an image of luxury and travellers were impressed by the wealth accumulated in the capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M532_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-M532-279">[276]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Fourth Crusade resulted in the disruption of Byzantine manufacturing and the commercial dominance of the Western Europeans in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">eastern Mediterranean</a>, events that amounted to an economic catastrophe for the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M532_279-1"><a href="#cite_note-M532-279">[276]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Palaiologos" title="Palaiologos">Palaiologoi</a> tried to revive the economy, but the late Byzantine state did not gain full control of either the foreign or domestic economic forces. Gradually, Constantinople also lost its influence on the modalities of trade and the price mechanisms, and its control over the outflow of precious metals and, according to some scholars, even over the minting of coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M806_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-M806-280">[277]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the economic foundations of Byzantium was trade, fostered by the maritime character of the empire. Textiles must have been by far the most important item of export; <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silks</a> were certainly imported into Egypt and appeared also in Bulgaria, and the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L723_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-L723-281">[278]</a></sup> The state strictly controlled both the internal and the international trade, and retained the monopoly of issuing <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_coinage" title="Byzantine coinage">coinage</a>, maintaining a durable and flexible monetary system adaptable to trade needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L3-4_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-L3-4-282">[279]</a></sup>
</p><p>The government attempted to exercise formal control over interest rates and set the parameters for the activity of the <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> and corporations, in which it had a special interest. The emperor and his officials intervened at times of crisis to ensure the provisioning of the capital, and to keep down the price of cereals. Finally, the government often collected part of the surplus through taxation, and put it back into circulation, through redistribution in the form of salaries to state officials, or in the form of investment in public works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L3-4_282-1"><a href="#cite_note-L3-4-282">[279]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Rome" title="Third Rome">Third Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">Greek scholars in the Renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2900" data-file-width="2744" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg/250px-Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg/375px-Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg/500px-Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Christ Pantocrator mosaic in <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, circa 1261</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar nomobile nowraplinks hlist" style="width:18em"><tbody><tr><th class="sidebar-title"><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Byzantine culture</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_church_in_Nahariya_-_Peacocks_Mosaic.jpg"><img alt="Byzantine church in Nahariya - Peacocks Mosaic.jpg" data-file-height="763" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="86" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Byzantine_church_in_Nahariya_-_Peacocks_Mosaic.jpg/170px-Byzantine_church_in_Nahariya_-_Peacocks_Mosaic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Byzantine_church_in_Nahariya_-_Peacocks_Mosaic.jpg/255px-Byzantine_church_in_Nahariya_-_Peacocks_Mosaic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Byzantine_church_in_Nahariya_-_Peacocks_Mosaic.jpg/340px-Byzantine_church_in_Nahariya_-_Peacocks_Mosaic.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Byzantine_culture" title="Template:Byzantine culture"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Byzantine_culture" title="Template talk:Byzantine culture"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Byzantine_culture&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Byzantium has been often identified with absolutism, orthodox spirituality, orientalism and exoticism, while the terms "Byzantine" and "Byzantinism" have been used as bywords for decadence, complex bureaucracy, and repression. Both Eastern and Western European authors have often perceived Byzantium as a body of religious, political, and philosophical ideas contrary to those of the West. Even in <a href="/wiki/History_of_modern_Greece" title="History of modern Greece">19th-century Greece</a>, the focus was mainly on the classical past, while Byzantine tradition had been associated with negative connotations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A1_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-A1-283">[280]</a></sup>
</p><p>This traditional approach towards Byzantium has been partially or wholly disputed and revised by modern studies, which focus on the positive aspects of Byzantine culture and legacy. <a href="/wiki/Averil_Cameron" title="Averil Cameron">Averil Cameron</a> regards as undeniable the Byzantine contribution to the formation of medieval Europe, and both Cameron and Obolensky recognise the major role of Byzantium in shaping Orthodoxy, which in turn occupies a central position in the history, societies and culture of Greece, Romania, Bulgaria, Russia, Georgia, Serbia and other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[281]</a></sup> The Byzantines also preserved and copied classical manuscripts, and they are thus regarded as transmitters of classical knowledge, as important contributors to modern European civilisation, and as precursors of both <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a> and Slavic-Orthodox culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C311_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-C311-285">[282]</a></sup> Some scholars focused on the positive aspects of Byzantine culture and legacy, French historian <a href="/wiki/Charles_Diehl" title="Charles Diehl">Charles Diehl</a> describes the Byzantine Empire by saying:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Byzantium created a brilliant culture, may be, the most brilliant during the whole Middle Ages, doubtlessly the only one existing in Christian Europe before the XI century. For many years, Constantinople remained the sole grand city of Christian Europe ranking second to none in splendour. Byzantium literature and art exerted a significant impact on peoples around it. The monuments and majestic works of art, remaining after it, show us the whole lustre of byzantine culture. That's why Byzantium held a significant place in the history of Middle Ages and, one must admit it, a merited one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[283]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>As the only stable long-term state in Europe during the Middle Ages, Byzantium isolated Western Europe from newly emerging forces to the East. Constantly under attack, it distanced Western Europe from Persians, Arabs, Seljuk Turks, and for a time, the Ottomans. From a different perspective, since the 7th century, the evolution and constant reshaping of the Byzantine state were directly related to the respective progress of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C311_285-1"><a href="#cite_note-C311-285">[282]</a></sup> Following the conquest of Constantinople by the Ottoman Turks in 1453, Sultan Mehmed II took the title "<i>Kaysar-i Rûm</i>" (the Ottoman Turkish equivalent of <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">Caesar</a> of Rome), since he was determined to make the Ottoman Empire the heir of the Eastern Roman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[284]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Romania" was a popular name of the empire used mainly unofficially, which meant "land of the Romans".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[9]</a></sup> After 1081, it occasionally appears in official Byzantine documents as well. In 1204, the leaders of the Fourth Crusade gave the name <i>Romania</i> to their newly founded Latin Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup> The term does not refer to modern <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>.</span>
</li>

Harvard University Press. pp. 349–367. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780884024842" title="Special:BookSources/9780884024842"><bdi>9780884024842</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=From+%22Empire+of+the+Greeks%22+to+%22Byzantium%22&amp;rft.btitle=The+Invention+of+Byzantium+in+Early+Modern+Europe&amp;rft.pages=349-367&amp;rft.pub=Harvard+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2022&amp;rft.isbn=9780884024842&amp;rft.aulast=Kaldellis&amp;rft.aufirst=Anthony&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AByzantine+Empire"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fox, <a class="external text" href="http://www.romanity.org/htm/fox.01.en.what_if_anything_is_a_byzantine.01.htm" rel="nofollow">What, If Anything, Is a Byzantine?</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170816093022/http://www.romanity.org/htm/fox.01.en.what_if_anything_is_a_byzantine.01.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 16 August 2017 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>; <a href="#CITEREFRosser2011">Rosser 2011</a>, p. 1</span>

<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRosser2011">Rosser 2011</a>, p. 2.</span>

<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFKaldellis2022">Kaldellis, Anthony (2022). "From "Empire of the Greeks" to "Byzantium"<span class="cs1-kern-right"></span>".  In Ransohoff, Jake; Aschenbrenner, Nathanael (eds.). <i>The Invention of Byzantium in Early Modern Europe</i>. Harvard University Press. pp. 366–367. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780884024842" title="Special:BookSources/9780884024842"><bdi>9780884024842</bdi></a>. <q>The Crimean War had a profound—and unrecognized—impact by forging a new distinction between "Byzantine/Byzantium" and "Greek/Greece," in a context in which the "empire of the Greeks" had become a politically toxic concept to the Great Powers of Europe. In response, European intellectuals increasingly began to lean on the conceptually adjacent and neutral term Byzantium, in order to create a semantic bulwark between the acceptable national aspirations of the new Greek state, on the one hand, and its dangerous imperial fantasies and its (perceived) Russian patrons, on the other.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=From+%22Empire+of+the+Greeks%22+to+%22Byzantium%22&amp;rft.btitle=The+Invention+of+Byzantium+in+Early+Modern+Europe&amp;rft.pages=366-367&amp;rft.pub=Harvard+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2022&amp;rft.isbn=9780884024842&amp;rft.aulast=Kaldellis&amp;rft.aufirst=Anthony&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AByzantine+Empire"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFFossierSondheimer1997">Fossier &amp; Sondheimer 1997</a>, p. 104.</span>

They were well aware of their role as heirs of the Roman Empire, which for many centuries had united under a single government the whole Mediterranean world and much that was outside it."</span>

<li id='cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlewitz33:_Emperor_[[Michael_I_Rangabe|Michael_I]]_recognised_Charlemagne_as_an_"Emperor"._He_refused_to_recognise_him_as_a_"Roman_Emperor"_(a_title_which_Michael_reserved_for_himself_and_his_successors),_instead_recognising_him_as_the_"Emperor_of_the_Franks"-24'><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href='#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlewitz33:_Emperor_[[Michael_I_Rangabe|Michael_I]]_recognised_Charlemagne_as_an_"Emperor"._He_refused_to_recognise_him_as_a_"Roman_Emperor"_(a_title_which_Michael_reserved_for_himself_and_his_successors),_instead_recognising_him_as_the_"Emperor_of_the_Franks"_24-0'>^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKlewitz">Klewitz</a>, p. 33: Emperor <a href="/wiki/Michael_I_Rangabe" title="Michael I Rangabe">Michael I</a> recognised Charlemagne as an "Emperor". He refused to recognise him as a "Roman Emperor" (a title which Michael reserved for himself and his successors), instead recognising him as the "Emperor of the Franks".</span>

<li id="cite_note-199"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-199">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The faculty was composed exclusively of philosophers, scientists, rhetoricians, and <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philologists</a>; <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFTatakesMoutafakis,_Nicholas_J.2003">Tatakes, Vasileios N.; Moutafakis, Nicholas J. (2003). <i>Byzantine Philosophy</i>. Hackett Publishing. p. 189. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-87220-563-0" title="Special:BookSources/0-87220-563-0"><bdi>0-87220-563-0</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Byzantine+Philosophy&amp;rft.pages=189&amp;rft.pub=Hackett+Publishing&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=0-87220-563-0&amp;rft.aulast=Tatakes&amp;rft.aufirst=Vasileios+N.&amp;rft.au=Moutafakis%2C+Nicholas+J.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AByzantine+Empire"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The Formation of the Hellenic Christian Mind" by <a href="/wiki/Demetrios_Constantelos" title="Demetrios Constantelos">Demetrios Constantelos</a>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-89241-588-6" title="Special:BookSources/0-89241-588-6">0-89241-588-6</a>: "The fifth century marked a definite turning point in Byzantine higher education. Theodosios ΙΙ founded in 425 a major university with 31 chairs for law, philosophy, medicine, arithmetic, geometry, astronomy, music, rhetoric and other subjects. Fifteen chairs were assigned to Latin and 16 to Greek. The university was reorganized by Michael III (842–867) and flourished down to the fourteenth century".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-201"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-201">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">John H. Rosser, <i>Historical Dictionary of Byzantium</i>, Scarecrow Press, 2001, p. xxx.</span>

<li id="cite_note-202"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-202">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Aleksandr Petrovich Kazhdan, Annabel Jane Wharton, <i>Change in Byzantine Culture in the Eleventh and Twelfth Centuries</i>, University of California Press, 1985, p. 122.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Loukaki_1997,_1553-203"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Loukaki_1997,_1553_203-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Marina Loukaki: "Université. Domaine byzantin", in: <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dictionnaire_encyclop%C3%A9dique_du_Moyen_%C3%82ge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dictionnaire encyclopédique du Moyen Âge (page does not exist)">Dictionnaire encyclopédique du Moyen Âge</a></i>, Vol. 2, Éditions du Cerf, Paris, 1997, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/2-204-05866-1" title="Special:BookSources/2-204-05866-1">2-204-05866-1</a>, p. 1553: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Le nom "université" désigne au Moyen Âge occidental une organisation corporative des élèves et des maîtres, avec ses fonctions et privilèges, qui cultive un ensemble d'études supérieures. L'existence d'une telle institution est fort contestée pour Byzance. Seule l'école de Constantinople sous Théodose Il peut être prise pour une institution universitaire. Par la loi de 425, l'empereur a établi l'"université de Constantinople", avec 31 professeurs rémunérés par l'État qui jouissaient du monopole des cours publics.</p></blockquote></span>
</li>
